1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""

reasoning
I'm mapping out the architecture for a fused kernel that handles the entire 4-layer forward pass in a single decode step. Each of the 4 blocks contains 3 KDA layers plus 1 MLA layer, all with mixture-of-experts routing. The projections are substantial—q, k, v, and gating projections each expand from 2304 to 4096 dimensions using int4 quantization, while beta projections go to 32 dimensions in bf16, and there's a convolution component involved. The MLA blocks have their own projection scheme with separate key-value projections and a growing cache, while the MoE router selects from 64 experts plus a shared expert using top-8 selection. The real challenge is enforcing this as a true megakernel—a single kernel launch that handles all sequential dependencies across layers without multiple launches. This requires either a persistent kernel with grid size 1 (which would be too slow) or cross-CTA synchronization using cooperative groups or global memory barriers. I'm considering whether to use CUDA with cooperative kernel launches and grid-wide barriers, which is cleaner, or Triton with atomic-based spinlock barriers for synchronization between phases. Both are feasible, though the CUDA approach feels more reliable. But there's a bigger issue: the MLA attention projects the entire cached context through kv_b at every step—for a 16384-token cache that's a massive matmul, around 137 GFLOPs per forward pass. The baseline does this too, but I need to think about whether there's a smarter way to absorb or optimize this computation. Working through the tensor layout for the absorbed computation: the kv_b projection outputs both nope-k and v components per head, so I need to carefully track which weight indices correspond to which dimensions. The key insight is that I can absorb the weight matrix multiplication into q_abs by precomputing how each cache element contributes to the attention scores, combining both the nope dot product and rope components. Computing the attention context by multiplying attention weights with the cached key-value embeddings (a [L,32]^T × [L,512] operation), then projecting through the value weights to get output embeddings—both around 2M MACs each. The scoring step recomputes dot products between queries and cached keys, requiring another pass over the cache, so the MLA attention makes two full sweeps through the c_kv data. Comparing to the baseline approach, this saves massive memory bandwidth at long context since we're only materializing a compressed 32MB cache instead of projecting the entire 137GB key-value buffer. Then breaking down the per-layer memory footprint: the query, key, value, and gating projections are quantized to int4 (about 4.7MB each, totaling ~19MB), the output projection adds another 4.7MB, and the quantization scales and zero points add roughly 3MB more, plus the KDA state matrices. For the MLA layers, the projections are larger—query at 7.1MB, the key-value adapter at 0.66MB, the key-value buffer at 2.1MB, and output at 4.7MB—bringing each layer to about 14.6MB before accounting for cache traffic. The MoE layers are the heaviest, with nine experts (eight routed plus one shared) where each expert's gate and up projections plus the down projection total around 3.54MB, multiplied across all nine experts gives roughly 32MB per layer, then adding the quantization metadata on top. Across four MoE layers that's about 127MB total, so each forward step materializes roughly 220MB of weights and activations. At the memory bandwidth of 1.8TB/s, that's a theoretical floor around 0.12ms, whereas the baseline approach of materializing bf16 weights plus fp32 overhead probably runs 1.5-3ms, suggesting a potential 5-15x speedup is achievable. For implementation, I'm weighing CUDA C++ with cooperative launches and grid synchronization against Triton—a single megakernel with internal barriers gives more control, though writing the full CUDA kernel with fused int4 dequantization, GEMV, and convolution operations is substantial. With Triton, I could use a persistent kernel where each program acts as a worker and a global barrier via atomics coordinates between phases, though I need to be careful about forward progress guarantees—the grid size can't exceed the number of SMs that can run concurrently, which on the RTX PRO 6000 with 188 SMs gives me some headroom. CUDA C++ would give me full control with warp-level primitives and PTX if needed, but that's a substantial undertaking—probably 2000+ lines. Triton is faster to write, especially for the dequant GEMV kernel, and global barriers via atomic spin loops are a proven pattern in practice. The catch with Triton is that the compiler might reorder loads across the spin loop, so I'd need to use atomic operations with acquire-release semantics and volatile loads to ensure correctness around the memory model. CUDA is safer here—__threadfence() and cooperative groups with grid.sync() give me explicit ordering guarantees. I'll go with CUDA and cooperative launch, then map out the kernel phases. For the KDA layer, I'm thinking through the sequence: first compute rmsnorm on x to get xn (2304 elements), then run the GEMV. The question is whether each CTA computes rmsnorm redundantly or if I precompute it globally. Redundant computation per CTA is cheap since 2304 floats fit in cache, but I could also precompute and store xn globally to avoid duplication across phases. Now I'm counting the synchronization overhead — grid.sync runs in 1-3 microseconds, and with 30-40 syncs per step that's 50-100 microseconds total, which cuts into the 120-microsecond floor. I need to minimize barriers where possible. For Phase A, I'm computing the q, k, v, g GEMVs (2304→4096 four times) plus beta (2304→32), all independent once xn is ready. I could fold rmsnorm directly into the GEMV computation — computing xn = x * rms * w per CTA by reading x and the attention norm weight, which is negligible compared to the 4.7MB weight matrices. Phase B handles the short convolution with silu activation over 4 taps using the conv window state, then updates the KDA state by scaling S with exp(g). The state update per head multiplies the 128×128 state matrix by exp(g[h]), and the prediction computes S^T k — that's 32 heads × 16384 elements = 512K fp32 values, so 4MB read-write. The delta rule needs k, v, g, and beta after convolution applies to them, though g and beta skip the conv step. Phase C handles the output projection and residual connection, then Phase D tackles the MoE router—a small 2304→64 GEMV with topk-8 selection and softmax normalization. I'm weighing whether to have one CTA compute the router redundantly across all CTAs (which would be expensive at 54MB of traffic) versus having a single CTA or small group compute it while others wait at the barrier. The constraint is that the router needs the post-residual h value, which only becomes available after Phase C completes, so there's no opportunity to overlap these phases. Phase E then runs the expert GEMVs—gate/up projections for each of the 9 experts followed by the down projection—split into two sub-phases since the gate/up operations depend on the router's expert selection while the down projection depends on the expert outputs. Across a single KDA block, this creates roughly 6 synchronization barriers (phases A through F), and with 4 blocks that's around 24 barriers total, plus additional overhead from the MLA layers. Even accounting for the ~2 microsecond cost per barrier, the cumulative overhead reaches maybe 60 microseconds, which is noticeable but still small relative to the baseline compute time of several milliseconds. Rather than aggressively optimize barrier count right now, I'll measure the actual impact later. For reducing barrier overhead, I can use cooperative groups' grid.sync() which runs at around 1.5 microseconds with 188 CTAs, which should be acceptable. Merging the router phase into phase C is tricky since the router needs the full hidden state across all 2304 dimensions, which forces a barrier anyway. Computing it redundantly in phase E would waste too much work, so I'll stick with keeping phase D separate but give its minimal work to CTA 0 while others wait at the barrier. For the MLA block, I'm mapping out the phases: phase A handles rmsnorm and projections for q and kv, phase B applies rope and appends to the cache buffer, and I need to figure out the remaining phases and their barrier points. The key challenge is managing the cache buffer efficiently—I could preallocate a larger tensor and use views into it rather than reallocating on each step, which avoids the expensive copy overhead. The state dict can hold a view of this preallocated buffer, so st["c_kv"] points to the right slice of the larger allocation. Since _run reinitializes state fresh for each trial, the one-time copy of the initial cache into the capacity buffer happens once per batch of 16-32 steps, making it negligible. The kernel then writes the new row at position L, and Python-side view creation keeps st["c_kv"] pointing to the updated range without additional overhead. For the query-key bias computation, I need to dequantize the k-part weight columns (512×4096 int4, about 1MB) and compute a GEMV where each head's query absolute values dot with the corresponding dequantized weight row. I can parallelize this across both the cache dimension and heads, with the weight traffic staying at 1MB per phase. Now moving to attention scoring: I'm splitting the sequence length L across CTAs and using a two-pass flash-decode approach where each CTA processes its L-chunk to compute local max, sum-exp, and weighted context for all 32 heads. The score for each position and head combines the cache-key dot product with the rope-key dot product. Each CTA loads the cache keys (1KB per position) and performs 16K multiply-accumulates per position to compute all 32 head scores, with query absolute values already in registers. For the output accumulation, I'm distributing heads across warps—each warp handles 4 heads and accumulates their context contributions (4×512 fp32 values) in registers, which fits within the 64 registers per lane. After processing each chunk, a barrier synchronizes before the final reduction phase to rescale across chunks and sum the partial contexts. Then comes the value projection: computing o[h,e] by multiplying the finalized context with the value weights (about 1MB), followed by the output projection that maps the 4096-dimensional o back to 2304 dimensions. I can fuse the value GEMV and output projection into a single phase since the output projection depends on the full o vector. This gives me phases for partial attention, reduction with value projection, output projection with residual, and then the router and MoE layers afterward. The total barrier count works out to around 26 per step. For the softmax, I'm sticking with the two-phase flash-style approach rather than trying a single global atomic pass. Now focusing on the core GEMV kernel: an int4 fused dequantization GEMV that takes bf16 input and produces bf16 output, with weights packed as uint8 and scales/zeros stored as bf16. The packed weight layout stores two 4-bit values per byte along the K dimension, with columns strided so consecutive output channels are consecutive in memory. The strategy is to have each thread block handle a tile of output columns (around 256), loop over K rows, and broadcast each input value to all threads. Each thread reads its corresponding packed weights coalesced across the warp, accumulating results in fp32 registers. Scales and zeros are reloaded per group of 128 rows. The read pattern streams row-major over the full weight tile for perfect efficiency, and I'm vectorizing by having each thread handle multiple columns' worth of nibbles per row. I'll write a generic device function that takes the input, weights, scales, zeros, dimensions, and output column range, then let the phase code assign each CTA its slice of the total columns. For example, in Phase A with four GEMVs totaling 16,384 columns, I'd distribute roughly 88 columns per CTA across 188 blocks. Each CTA handles a column tile of 64–128 columns, loading the full K=2304 input into shared memory once, then streaming through its assigned weight columns. With 32 threads per warp reading 4 columns at a time (since each byte encodes 2 K-values), a 128-column tile aligns nicely with warp-level coalescing. I'll split K across 8 warps so each accumulates partial sums over its K/8 chunk for all 128 columns, then reduce the results in shared memory before writing out. s can be applied per group, accumulating x0*lo + x1*hi separately from the (x0+x1) sum which is column-independent. Then I'm considering whether integer math with dp4a could work, but since activations are floats, I'd need to convert nibbles to float anyway—so the FP path with int-to-float conversion and FMA operations is the practical approach. Now I'm analyzing the memory and compute tradeoffs: 220MB of weights becomes 440M nibbles, which translates to roughly 2.2G flops plus 1.3G conversions. On a GPU with ~100 TFLOP fp32 throughput, the conversion bottleneck is the real constraint—int-to-float conversion runs at quarter rate on consumer hardware, so 440M conversions at full GPU capacity would take around 56 microseconds, which is noticeable overhead. A better approach is using LOP3-based bit manipulation to convert int4s to bf16 or fp16 directly via the classic magic number trick from Marlin and AWQ—essentially or-ing with a magic exponent and subtracting to get the final float representation. I'm planning to implement the AWQ dequantization kernel using half2 math to process column pairs, which gives a ~2x+ speedup. The idea is to load 32-bit words containing 8 nibbles (representing 4 columns × 2 k-values), then convert them to half2 values using the bit manipulation approach rather than explicit float conversions. Extracting the nibbles with bit shifts to get the column indices, then deciding whether to accumulate in half or full precision—there's a risk of overflow if I sum 1024-scaled values across 128 elements in fp16, so I'm leaning toward fp32 accumulation with half2 FMA operations for efficiency. Considering whether to accumulate in fp32 instead to avoid precision loss, though that adds conversion overhead. The simpler approach might be using half-precision FMA with separate tracking of the sum for zero-handling, accumulating products per group while folding the offset into the computation to avoid extra operations per element. Exploring how to fold the offset into the accumulation using half2 operations — the x-sum trick lets me avoid explicit subtraction if I structure the HSUB2 or HFMA2 carefully, extracting the weight with the magic constant baked in and letting the fused multiply-add handle the offset implicitly. I'm concerned about overflow risks when numeric stress tests scale activations large, so I need to use explicit half-precision subtraction per half2 element. Looking at the operation count: each byte-quad processes 8 products with about 14 operations total, which across 55 million uint32 chunks gives roughly 770 million operations—hitting around 50% of the theoretical half-precision throughput on consumer Blackwell hardware. Actually, wait—I'm re-reading the check.py runner and it doesn't actually include numeric stress testing despite what was mentioned in the documentation. The mega check.py just does simple cosine similarity across different seeds and context lengths. Still, I should be careful about fp16 range since after RMSnorm the activations are normalized to unit variance with weights around 1, so values stay bounded under 5, and the dot product stays under 75 even summed across 128 dimensions. The KDA convolution and MLA layers use fp32 in the reference implementation, so I'll accumulate the GEMV operations per-group in half2 and cross-group in fp32—that's fine for maintaining 0.98 cosine similarity. I considered a simpler dequantization approach using element-wise conversions from int to float per nibble, but the math shows that would take around 50 microseconds with 440 million conversions, whereas the half2 path is faster. I'll go with the half2 approach. For the GEMV inputs, I need to load x into shared memory as half-precision (converting from bf16), which is straightforward for 2304 elements per CTA, then broadcast it as half2 pairs for the actual computation. Now structuring the warp iteration: each warp processes 2 k-values at a time, loading 128 bytes (128 columns) per row across 32 lanes with 4 columns per lane. Each lane maintains 4 fp32 accumulators for per-group and cross-group results. The warp loops through all K/2 rows (1152 total for K=2304), so each warp owns its 128 columns completely without needing cross-warp reduction. With 8 warps per CTA, that covers 1024 columns total. But this creates a utilization problem: for KDA-A with 16384 columns, we'd need 128 column tiles of 128 each, yet we have 188 CTAs × 8 warps = 1504 warps available. That means only 128 warps stay busy while the rest idle during phase A. The solution is to split K across warps within a CTA and use shared memory reduction to combine results across the warp group. Reconsidering the tile granularity: if I use 64-column tiles instead, that gives 256 tiles total, which exceeds our 188 CTAs and creates some load imbalance around 32%, but better utilizes the hardware. The real constraint is memory bandwidth saturation—with only 18.9MB of weights for KDA-A, I need each CTA streaming efficiently rather than sitting idle. I'm exploring a 64-column tile approach where each warp loads uint16 values (2 bytes per lane), then pivoting to a larger scheme: a 256-thread CTA handling a 128-column tile with K distributed across 8 warps, where each warp processes about 144 rows and reads 128 bytes per row with full coalescing. The shared memory reduction across warps uses 4KB for 128 columns of fp32 accumulators, and the per-tile compute density works out to roughly 0.14MB of int4 weights across 188 CTAs. Now I'm considering dynamic tiling for MoE phases—gate and up layers need 144 tiles, down needs 162, but the output projection becomes problematic: with N=2304 and K=4096, I'd only have 18 CTAs busy, which is terrible utilization. I need to split K across CTAs for these wide-K narrow-N GEMVs and use cross-CTA reduction with atomicAdd or a two-phase reduce strategy. For a simpler generic approach, I'm thinking of making each GEMV work-unit a combination of column tile (128 cols) and K-chunk, sized so the total units are 2-4× the number of CTAs, with partial results accumulated into fp32 scratch via atomicAdd and scratch zeroed in the prior phase to avoid extra overhead. The post-ops like bias, silu, residual, and conv happen in the next phase when y is read anyway, so that's naturally pipelined. The atomicAdd ordering is fine since grid.sync has grid-wide release-acquire semantics, and fp32 precision handles nondeterministic accumulation order without issues. The main question is whether to dedicate a tail of each phase's CTA work for zeroing, or zero all scratch accumulators at the kernel start. Since scratch buffers are reused across layers, I'm thinking phase B's consumers could zero the buffer parts they just read after consuming them—they're writing conv outputs elsewhere anyway. This keeps the buffer clean for the next layer. The write-after-read hazard is safe since the same thread reads then zeros the same address, and in phase B each buffer element is read by exactly one consumer (the conv consumer reads only its channel slice, and KDA state updates read k,v through their own paths). I'm also considering a cleaner alternative: split-K with per-chunk scratch slots and reduction in the consuming phase. Instead of atomics and zeroing, consumers would sum a small number of partials (2-4 chunks) from y_partial[chunk, N]—trivial overhead since they're already reading y. This is deterministic, avoids atomics entirely, and the scratch memory cost is negligible. The main constraint is deciding on a fixed chunk count across all GEMVs. For the qkvg kernel with K=2304 (18 groups of 128), splitting into 4 chunks gives uneven group boundaries, but that's manageable by computing ranges per group index. I'm leaning toward statically choosing chunk counts per kernel type to keep things simple. I need to handle the ragged tail case where the output dimension isn't a clean multiple of 128, and for the kv_b weight used in q_abs computation, I'm reading rows indexed by output dimension c and performing a dot product with the broadcasted q values across all 32 heads, producing 512 output values per head. For each row c2, I'm loading 128 bytes across the d-range for one head, then using warps to process these bytes where each lane handles 4 d-values and reduces the dot product to produce two outputs (the low and high nibble pairs) per head per row. For the Wv GEMV, I'm treating each of the 32 heads as a separate matrix multiply with K=512 and N=128 per head, which breaks down into 64 work units across 2 chunks—about 1MB of traffic, totally manageable. The MLA output projection is a larger operation at K=4096, N=2304 requiring 144 units. For attention over the cache with sequence lengths up to 16384, I'm partitioning the work across L-chunks and CTAs, reading the 16MB cache twice for scoring and context passes, though there might be a way to fuse these into a single pass. Now I'm weighing whether to use online softmax with flash-decode style computation—each CTA processes its L-chunk with running max/sum and context accumulation, storing per-chunk statistics and context in scratch memory. This approach writes about 12MB of intermediate state across 188 chunks, then needs a reduction phase reading those partials back, totaling around 40MB of traffic. Comparing that to the two-pass approach of reading the cache twice (32MB) plus small score scratch (2MB), the two-pass method actually comes out ahead on bandwidth. But with fewer chunks to saturate bandwidth, the one-pass partial context becomes more competitive at 12MB, though it still has the overhead of the reduction phase computing weighted sums of context across chunks using the global max and sum normalization. Exploring different partitioning strategies for the second pass — whether to split work by output columns (each CTA handles a slice of context) or by input chunks (each CTA processes one L-chunk and uses atomic operations to accumulate into the shared context buffer). The column-based approach requires re-reading all scores, while the chunk-based approach with atomicAdd keeps memory access more localized. Partitioning the second pass differently as a matrix multiplication of context vectors — each CTA handles a quadrant of heads and a tile of channels, reading all the key-value rows for that tile. The memory access pattern is efficient since each CTA reads contiguous 256-byte chunks from the key-value rows, which coalesces well across the four channel tiles needed. However, this approach causes each channel tile to be read by multiple head-quadrant CTAs, multiplying the total memory traffic to 64MB — worse than the atomic-based approach that reads the key-value matrix exactly once with 188-way parallelism. The atomic version is cleaner here. For the first pass computing scores, I need the query absolute values (a 64KB matrix) which each CTA reads from global memory, likely benefiting from L2 caching. Now moving into the KDA phase B: each head maintains a 128×128 state matrix in fp32 (64KB), and with 32 CTAs assigned per head, the work involves reading and writing that state plus a small output vector — roughly 15 microseconds per CTA at current bandwidth. I can optimize further by splitting work per column tile of the state: each column's updates are independent, so I can compute column sums and apply the update rule in parallel, where each column j gets updated with scaled key-value contributions and produces its output contribution. This gives me 4096 independent columns across all heads, which maps nicely to 128 CTAs if each handles a head and a block of 32 columns. But there's a dependency issue: the convolution that produces q, k, v must run first in phase B, and then the state update needs those conv outputs. If both happen in phase B, I need to figure out which CTA computes the convolution for each head's channels so the state-update CTA can access them efficiently. The convolution itself is cheap—just 4 taps per channel across 3×128 channels—but the tricky part is the window state update. Each CTA would be shifting and writing to the same window buffer, which creates a race condition if one CTA reads while another writes. Even though the writes are identical values, I need to be careful about the ordering of the in-place shift operation where each row gets replaced by the next row and the new values append at the end. The real issue is that there's a race condition when multiple CTAs try to update the same row — CTA A might write row1 with a value derived from row2, but CTA B could read row1 before or after that write, getting inconsistent data. The fundamental problem is that convolution and window updates need to be owned by a single CTA per channel to avoid these conflicts, but since everything happens in the same phase, there's no way to enforce ordering between the convolution computation and the state update that depends on it. I'm considering two approaches: either split this into separate phases with a barrier between convolution output and state updates, or use a double-buffered window system where we pre-allocate two buffers and ping-pong between them instead of allocating new tensors each step, which would avoid the Python overhead of repeated allocations. Each CTA handles its head and j-block, redundantly computing the convolution locally from the old window and phase-A scratch to get the new q', k', v' values, then updates the state tensor in place for just its assigned columns — reads and writes stay within the CTA's exclusive region, keeping everything race-free in a single phase-B kernel. Phase A also produces g and beta via small GEMVs alongside the q, k, v pre-convolution outputs, both needed downstream. Then phase C wraps up with the output projection GEMV (4096→2304 channels) and adds the residual connection. The tricky part is managing the residual: phase C writes partial sums from split-K chunks, but multiple downstream consumers need the final h = x + attn_out (the router in phase D, and the gate/up GEMVs in phase E both need rmsnorm(h)). Rather than have each consumer independently reduce and add the residual, I'm considering whether phase C itself should handle the reduction and residual addition, or if phase D should take on that responsibility as a separate reduction step before routing. A cleaner approach: pre-initialize an h-accumulator buffer with x during phase B (since x is already finalized), then have phase C atomically add its o_proj partials into this buffer. After the barrier, h_buf contains the complete h, which phase D can read directly for the router computation. Similarly for the MoE output path: phase F atomically adds into an x_next buffer that was pre-loaded with h during phase E, so x_next = h + moe_out is ready for the next iteration. The MoE down-GEMV results get multiplied by the expert weight w_j (available at phase F from the router output), so the atomic additions work correctly. The reference implementation accumulates MoE outputs in fp32 then casts to bf16 before adding to h, while I'm keeping everything in fp32 accumulation and casting only at the final output—with a cosine similarity of 0.98, the precision difference is negligible. Now looking at the synchronization barriers across the KDA blocks: there's one barrier per phase (A through F for the router), totaling 6 barriers per block. The router phase itself is tiny—just one CTA handling a 64×2304 GEMV in bf16 plus softmax and top-8 selection—while other CTAs sit idle for a couple microseconds, which is acceptable. I'm considering whether to merge phases, like pulling the router into phase E where gate/up runs, but the gate/up kernels need the expert IDs from the router output, so they'd have to spin-wait on a flag anyway—that's just another barrier in disguise. Across 4 blocks plus MLA overhead, I'm looking at roughly 28 barriers total, which could add up to 40-80 microseconds of synchronization cost. I'm exploring alternatives: replicating the router computation across all CTAs redundantly would require each CTA to read 288KB, totaling 54MB bandwidth—way too expensive at 30 microseconds just for that. Another angle is computing router logits from h the moment h is ready at the end of phase C, but merging that into phase C itself doesn't work without intra-phase ordering. The better path forward is to reduce barrier latency itself by implementing a lightweight global barrier using an atomic counter and spin-wait on a flag. At 188 CTAs, the cooperative grid sync should hit around 2 microseconds per barrier, so 28 barriers across the full step would be roughly 56 microseconds. With context length 2048, I'm estimating the total step time at 200-250 microseconds, compared to a baseline of 2.5-4 milliseconds (accounting for bf16 dequantization of 220MB expanding to 880MB, plus Python and kernel launch overhead across ~100 launches). That's a 12-18x speedup, which is solid enough to start iterating on. I'm exploring whether I can reduce the barrier count by restructuring the phases differently — maybe computing all 64 experts' gate and up projections together, but that's 226MB, which doesn't fit. I'm also considering whether I can move the router computation earlier by exploiting linearity, but rmsnorm isn't linear, so that's a dead end. For now I'll accept the ~28 barriers and optimize later. I'm wondering if I can merge the router into phase E as a cheap sequential prelude that all CTAs execute, or precompute partial router logits during phase C, but the router needs the hidden state which isn't available until later. Now I'm working through the actual phase D logistics: spreading the 64 router logits across 8 CTAs (64 warps) to compute them in parallel, but then topk needs all 64 logits available, which creates another cross-CTA barrier. Computing all logits in a single CTA is too slow at one CTA's bandwidth — 288KB of weights takes ~19 microseconds, which is prohibitive. I'm exploring whether I can keep the router weights resident in shared memory across the entire persistent kernel instead of reloading them each phase. The full weight matrix across 4 layers is 1.15MB, which fits in the total GPU shared memory budget if I distribute slices across CTAs — each CTA could hold about 6KB of its assigned weight slice and load it once at kernel start. But this still leaves the problem of needing cross-CTA reduction to gather all logits for topk. I'm also considering a precision-focused approach: computing logits hierarchically within a single CTA during phase D1, splitting the 288KB weight matrix across 8 warps so each handles 36KB. Since these weights are accessed every step, they should stay resident in L2 cache (which has 128MB on this GPU), giving much higher bandwidth than main memory. The full 220MB model doesn't fit in L2, but the actively used weights do. Beta projection and MLA phase breakdown... For the cache initialization, I'm deciding between doing a one-time tensor copy on the Python side versus handling it inside the kernel itself. A separate cudaMemcpy call risks breaking the "single kernel per step" requirement, so it's safer to add a flag that triggers the cache copy during phase A on the first step only—adds about 20 microseconds initially but amortizes to roughly 1 microsecond per step across a trajectory. For the KDA convolution windows, I'm using ping-pong buffers where the first step reads directly from the user's input tensors and writes to buffer A, then subsequent steps alternate between buffers as Python swaps the pointers passed in. The state views need to maintain the right shape (3, 4096) to work correctly. Now I'm verifying the state tensor handling—checking that one step produces the correct outputs by comparing the solution state's S and c_kv fields. The S tensor updates in-place, and c_kv gets a view into the cache buffer up to length L. The k_rope follows the same pattern, and the cq/ck/cv views correctly reference the ping-pong buffers. For multi-step chains, I'm benchmarking to ensure the state carries forward properly, with each step reading pointers from the current state tensors. This approach works even if the user provides fresh tensors at any point. For the RoPE computation, I need the position index L and I'm calculating cos/sin values in-kernel using the angle formula (pos × inv_freq). The challenge is precision—for large position values up to 16400 with inverse frequencies around 1, the angle can reach 16400 radians, and the built-in __sinf/__cosf functions lose accuracy at those magnitudes, so I'm switching to the standard sinf/cosf for better precision. Writing the kernel logic... The output projection accumulates into a buffer that gets residually added back, and I'm planning to use alternating buffers per layer so the router can copy the hidden state to the next layer's input while other CTAs handle idle work — essentially treating the attention output buffer and MoE accumulator as a ping-pong pair to avoid synchronization overhead. I'm settling on allocating five residual buffers for layer inputs and four attention hidden buffers, all in fp32. The first layer's phase A loads the input hidden state (converting from bf16 to fp32) into the first residual buffer, and the final output needs to convert back to bf16 — but that conversion can't be a separate kernel launch since the step must invoke exactly one kernel. So I need to add a tail micro-phase that handles the cast after the atomicAdd barrier completes. The KV cache writes are already bf16, and the attention state S stays fp32 in-place with the same tensor pointer across steps. For the router's topk selection, I'm using torch.topk to pick the 8 experts — ties are unlikely with random floats, so the selection should be deterministic — and then normalizing the weights with the same formula to ensure consistency. Now I'm thinking through the kernel launch strategy: a single cooperative kernel with 256 threads per CTA, either 188 CTAs (one per SM) or 376 CTAs (two per SM) for better latency hiding. The KDA state tile for each CTA is 128×32 fp32, which is 16KB — I can fit this in registers or shared memory, mapping threads to column ranges of the state matrix. For the S tile (128×32), I need 64KB of shared memory per CTA. Given that Blackwell has 128KB per SM but only 100KB usable per CTA, allocating 64KB dynamic shared memory limits me to one CTA per SM. With a grid of 188 and cooperative launch enabled, this works as long as every CTA's resource requirements fit the constraint — the kernel's dynamic shared memory size is uniform across all CTAs, so 64KB per CTA means only one CTA can be resident per SM. Now I'm reconsidering the thread count: 256 threads per CTA with a grid of 188 gives 48K total threads, which might not saturate bandwidth effectively. Bandwidth saturation typically requires many outstanding loads per thread — around 8–16 concurrent loads per thread. A single CTA of 256 threads per SM is on the low side; bumping to 512 threads per CTA (16 warps) would be better. With 512 threads and 64KB shared memory, register pressure stays manageable — 64K registers per SM divided by 512 threads gives about 125 registers per thread, which is fine for the GEMV loops. This rebalances the grid to 188 CTAs with 16 warps each, totaling 3008 warps. Actually, let me reconsider whether we need the full head S tile in a single CTA. If I split by columns instead — each CTA handles a subset of j columns — then loading S becomes more efficient: threads load rows i=0..127 with columns from a specific j block, which gives coalesced 128-byte reads per row. I can map threads to (j, i-part) pairs — say 32 j's and 16 i-groups across 512 threads, with each thread looping over 8 i's, computing partial sums, then reducing over the 16 i-groups in shared memory to get pred[j]. After that, I scale S by the predicate. Now I'm working through the actual computation order: first decay S by exp(g[i]), compute pred from the decayed values, then update S with the beta term involving the difference (v[j] − pred[j]). Finally, compute output o[j] as the sum over i of the updated S values times q[i]. The key insight is that I can do two passes over the S tile while keeping values in registers — each thread holds 8 i × 1 j values (8 float32s = 8 registers), so in the first pass I load, decay, compute partial pred, reduce it, then update those 8 values in-place and compute partial o, reducing that too. S gets written back once at the end. The memory footprint for S is around 8MB for a 32×128×128 tile in float32. Checking the bandwidth: 2MB for S plus 2MB written per step per layer, times 3 layers gives 12MB total, which is manageable. For the decay computation, I realize that exp(−softplus(g_raw)) simplifies to sigmoid(−g_raw), so I can compute that directly rather than going through softplus and exp separately. The conv output gets silu activation, and I'm applying the standard KDA scaling of Dk^−0.5 to q, plus beta is computed as sigmoid(beta_proj). For the MoE layer, I'm computing softmax over all 64 router logits, then taking the top-8 probabilities and normalizing the weights by their sum with a small epsilon. The shared expert bypasses the router entirely with a fixed weight of 1. The MoE FFN applies silu and gating to the normalized input, then projects through the output weight, and while the reference implementation casts through bf16 but accumulates in fp32, my group-fp16 to fp32 accumulation should be equivalent. Now I'm looking at the weight dequantization path — the reference stores quantized nibbles (0-15) and bf16 zeros and scales, then dequantizes by computing (nibble - zero) * scale in bf16 before the float matmul, which means the weights get rounded to bf16 precision. My fused dequant path computes the same operation but in higher precision without that bf16 rounding step, which introduces a small numerical difference. The per-element relative error is around 2^-8 versus 2^-11, but the cosine similarity impact is negligible since the errors have random signs and average out to roughly 0.4% per weight, translating to about 0.4% output error divided by sqrt(N) — well within tolerance with cosine similarity staying above 0.98. For the fp16 accumulation in the GEMV inner loop, I'm checking whether the accumulator could overflow. The input x comes from RMSNorm (magnitude a few), the dequantized weights are at most 15, so each term stays under ~100, and a 128-element sum stays safely below the fp16 max of 65504 unless we hit pathological cases. For the MoE down-GEMV, the input is the gated output (silu(g) * u), which has magnitudes around N(0, ~1) scaled by the weight magnitude (~0.02), so the output should remain stable. I'm considering adding a compile-time-selectable fp32 accumulation fallback for extra safety, or accumulating per half-group of 64 elements in fp32 to halve the range risk and error with minimal cost overhead. Precision differences between fp16 and bf16 are manageable since intermediate values computed in fp32 get cast to fp16 with rounding that's close enough to the reference bf16 path. Now I'm mapping out the kernel structure: setting up a unified work queue per phase where each phase's unit scheduling is mostly hardcoded at compile time, with weight pointers passed as kernel arguments — there are a lot of them, especially across layers. Counting the pointers needed: each KDA layer requires 19 pointers (q, k, v, g, o quantized tensors with scales and zeros, plus beta, conv, and norm weights), multiplied by 3 layers gives 57. MLA layers add another 15 per layer. MoE layers are heavier at 19 pointers each across 4 layers. I need to fit all these into CUDA's 4KB parameter limit, possibly using a struct-of-pointers approach. Rather than passing 190 individual tensor arguments to the kernel, I'm thinking about caching the struct on the C++ side—initialize it once with `setup()` to store all device pointers globally, then each forward step only passes the changing pointers (around 12 per step: hidden states, conv windows in a ping-pong buffer). The C++ side just needs hidden state, output, layer count, and a flag for the first step to keep overhead minimal. The MLA cache gets mutated in place as a view of our buffer, which is fine since we're tracking identity. Python-side overhead per step comes from constructing those cache views through torch slicing and the wrapper call itself—probably 10-30 microseconds total, which is negligible compared to the actual computation. For attention, I'm using the standard scaling factor of (qk_nope + qk_rope)^-0.5 applied to the scores, and RoPE rotation follows the reference implementation. The query layout splits into nope and rope components per head, with the key-value compressed output containing the cached c_kv and k_rope components. I'm following the reference exactly here—no normalization on c_kv even though production MLA would have it. Now checking what happens in subsequent steps beyond the first one. Deciding between atomicAdd into zeroed fp32 scratch versus deterministic chunk-partials: the latter avoids complex zeroing discipline across phases. For qkvg with 2 chunks, each consumer reads 2 partial values per element. For o_proj with 8 chunks, the scratch holds 8 partial rows of 2304 elements; phase-E CTAs (gate/up GEMV) each need the full h after rmsnorm, so they sum across all 8 chunk partials. The L2 bandwidth concern: 74KB per CTA × ~150 CTAs = 11MB total, which is manageable since the L2 is hot. But h also serves as the residual input for preloading x_next, creating a same-phase race condition — phase D's router-CTA reduces h into h_final (writing), while other CTAs in phase D need to preload x_next = h_final + moe, which depends on that write completing first. To resolve this, I'm having all CTAs in phase D redundantly reduce their h-slices (288 elements each) and compute rmsnorm locally in shared memory, with each CTA calculating h_final into registers or smem. The L2 traffic is ~14MB (188 × 74KB), which at 4TB/s L2 bandwidth takes 3-4µs — acceptable. CTA0 handles the router using xn (already in smem), writes the ids and weights, but now I need to figure out which CTA writes h_final and xn_moe to global memory for phase E to consume. Merging phase D into E would let all CTAs redundantly compute x_n, but the router's topk must happen first since gate/up unit assignment depends on expert IDs — and those IDs get written in the same phase, creating a race condition. Position-based unit indexing doesn't help either. So phase D stays separate, keeping the 6 barriers per block. Alternatively, I could drop split-K for o_proj to sidestep the h-reduction overhead entirely, though that trades off parallelism across the 36 half-tiles. With K-split=4 I'm looking at around 2 microseconds per phase for the reduce step, which is roughly equivalent to the no-split approach but adds complexity. Key Technical Concepts: - W4A16 quantization: int4 packed 2-per-byte along K (even K → low nibble, odd K → high nibble), group-size 128 asymmetric dequant `(q - zero) * scale`, bf16 scales/zeros/activations. - Fused dequant-GEMV: batch-1 decode is weight-bandwidth bound; int4 stream = 0.25× bf16 bytes, but only if dequant fuses into the GEMV (baseline materializes bf16: int4 read + bf16 write + bf16 read ≈ 9× traffic). - KDA (Kimi Delta Attention / gated DeltaNet): 32 heads × dim 128; q/k/v projections → short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4, SiLU) → per-channel log-decay gate `g = -softplus(g_proj(x))`, per-head write strength `beta = sigmoid(beta_proj(x))`; state update `S = S*exp(g)[:, :, None]; pred = (S*k).sum(1); S += beta*k⊗(v-pred); o = (S*q*scale).sum(1)`. - MLA (multi-head latent attention): 32 heads, kv_lora 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE theta 10000 (interleaved even/odd pairs); growing cache `c_kv[L,512]` + `k_rope[L,64]`; reference re-expands whole cache through kv_b each step — a solution should use the absorbed-weight trick (score q_nope against cache via kv_b's k_nope part, weight-absorb for v). - MoE: router (tiny bf16 linear) → softmax → top-8, renormalized × routed_scaling 2.446, plus 1 shared expert; gate/up/down per expert are W4A16 (d=2304 → m=1024 → d). - RMSNorm eps 1e-6 in fp32; residual adds. - Megakernel design: single persistent grid launch with internal phases / grid-wide sync (e.g., Triton with atomic barriers or cooperative-groups CUDA), since layers are sequential dependencies. - Config constants: hidden=2304, kda_heads=32, kda_head_dim=128 (H*Dk=4096), short_conv=4, mla_heads=32, kv_lora=512, qk_nope=128, qk_rope=64, v_head=128, n_experts=64, n_active=8, n_shared=1, moe_inter=1024, group=128, pattern ("K","K","K","M"), EPS=1e-6. - Environment: RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell 96GB, driver 580.126.09, CUDA 13.0; GPU shared (another benchmark process holds ~26GB); harness uses uv, GPU lock wrappers. 3. Files and Code Sections: - `reference.py` (read in full — the correctness oracle): - Contract: `Model(cfg)`, `Model.step(hidden, state)`, `build_config(shape)`, `init_state(cfg, ctx, seed)`, `init_token(cfg, seed)`. - Pack format: ```python def _pack_int4(w_q): # (K,N) uint8 in [0,15] -> (K//2,N): even-K low nibble, odd-K high lo = w_q[0::2] & 0xF; hi = w_q[1::2] & 0xF return (lo | (hi << 4)).contiguous() def dequant(w_q, scales, zeros, K, group): wu = _unpack_int4(w_q, K).to(torch.bfloat16) s = scales.repeat_interleave(group, 0); z = zeros.repeat_interleave(group, 0) return (wu - z) * s ``` - `QuantLinear` buffers: `w_q` (in//2, out) uint8, `scales`/`zeros` (in//group, out) bf16. `QuantExperts` adds leading expert dim `n`. - KDA module: `q_proj/k_proj/v_proj/g_proj` QuantLinear(2304→4096), `beta_proj` nn.Linear(2304→32, bias=False, bf16), `conv_w` Parameter(3, 4096, 4) bf16, `o_proj` QuantLinear(4096→2304), `scale = 128**-0.5`. Short conv: `win = cat([prev, val[None]]); out = (win.float()*w).sum(0); silu(out)`; returns updated window `win[1:]`. - KDA step math (fp32): ```python g = (-F.softplus(self.g_proj(x).float())).view(H, Dk) beta = torch.sigmoid(self.beta_proj(x).float()) S = st["S"] * g.exp()[:, :, None] pred = (S * k[:, :, None]).sum(1) S = S + beta[:, None, None] * k[:, :, None] * (v - pred)[:, None, :] o = (S * q[:, :, None]).sum(1) # q pre-scaled by Dk**-0.5 ``` - MLA module: `q_proj` QuantLinear(2304→32*192), `kv_a` QuantLinear(2304→576), `kv_b` QuantLinear(512→32*256), `o_proj` QuantLinear(4096→2304), `scale = 192**-0.5`. RoPE: interleaved pairs, `pos = st["c_kv"].shape[0]` (before append). Cache append via `torch.cat`. Scores over full cache; softmax over L dim; output through o_proj. - MoE step: `probs = softmax(router(x).float()); w, idx = topk(probs, 8); w = w/(w.sum()+1e-9)*2.446`; per expert `h = silu(x@gate)*(x@up); out += w[j]*(h@down)`; plus shared expert unweighted. - Block: `h = x + attn.step(_rmsnorm(x, attn_norm), st); return h + moe.step(_rmsnorm(h, moe_norm))`. - `init_state`: KDA layers get `S` (32,128,128) fp32, `cq/ck/cv` (3, 4096) bf16; MLA gets `c_kv` (ctx,512) bf16, `k_rope` (ctx,64) bf16. `reset_parameters` uses CPU generator seed 1234. - `_rmsnorm`: fp32, eps 1e-6, weight multiply, cast back. - `_apply_rope`: even/odd interleave: `out[0::2] = even*cos - odd*sin; out[1::2] = odd*cos + even*sin`. - `baseline.py` (read in full — the timed floor): subclasses reference modules; bf16 matmuls against materialized dequant weights; batched expert dequant `_batch_wbf` gathering 8 experts to `[k, in, out]` bf16 then einsums. Must beat this. - `check.py` (read in full): imports reference, shapes, solution; AST-scans solution.py + local *.py modules it imports (recursively, incl. scratch/) for forbidden imports; loads ref weights strict=True into solution.Model; for seeds (0,1,2) × ctx (2048, 8192) compares cosine of `out`, `st[0]["S"]` (kda_state), `st[3]["c_kv"]` (mla_cache) ≥ 0.98; prints PASS; writes framework.txt label (first regex match of ptx / cuda_raw(`load_inline|__global__`) / triton(`import triton|@triton.jit`) / cudagraph / compile, else eager). - `benchmark.py` (read in full): for each shape builds ref/sol/base models, loads ref weights into both, correctness-gates one step (cosine ≥ 0.98, seed 7), then times `steps` autoregressive decode steps (best of 3 trials, after warmup of min(3,steps)); score = geomean of baseline/solution over shapes; prints `peak_fraction`. - `shapes.py`: `SHAPES = [{ctx 2048, steps 32}, {ctx 8192, steps 16}, {ctx 16384, steps 16}]`, `GRADING_SEEDS = (0,1,2)`. - `problem.yaml`: tolerance cos_sim 0.98; forbidden list (see above); megakernel constraint `launches_per_step: 1`, authenticity via judge; advisory tripwires [graph, compile, codegen, obfuscation]; num_perf_trials 3. - `solution.py` — NOT YET CREATED.
