Let me set up a baseline timing measurement first—I'll write a quick scratch script to measure latency at context length 2048 without needing the full benchmark infrastructure. I also need to verify the CUDA compiler version and check if cooperative kernel launches are supported through load_inline, then I can move forward with the CUDA C++ implementation. For the kernel design, I'm going to keep it straightforward: one cooperative kernel per step with a grid of 188 CTAs and 512 threads each, using dynamic shared memory around 64-100KB. Rather than building a unit descriptor table on the host side, I'll hardcode the phase logic directly in the kernel with a switch statement on the phase index and compute work assignments from blockIdx using compile-time constants. Since all dimensions are frozen (n_experts=64, D=2304, C=4096, H=32, Dk=128, G=128, NGD=18, E_ACT=9, M=1024, QN=128, QR=64, KVL=512, VH=1), I can use simple per-phase loops where each block handles its assigned units with straightforward phase-specific computation. Now I'm laying out the scratch buffers: x[5][D] for layer inputs in fp32, hattn[4][D] for attention residuals, and qkvg buffers for the KDA layers. I'm deciding whether to reuse qkvg across layers or allocate per-layer—allocating per-layer at 196KB total keeps zeroing simple and avoids coordination overhead between layers. I'm working through the zeroing strategy: phase Z zeros everything at the start of each step, which is a few MB taking around 5 microseconds. Most buffers get fully overwritten deterministically so they don't need explicit zeroing, but the gate/up atomicAdd targets for the four layers add up to about 295KB and need careful handling depending on whether I'm using chunk=1. For qkvg with K-chunks=2, I need atomics, but if I keep chunk=1 with 128 units each handling their column tiles exclusively, I can use normal stores without atomics or zeroing. The o_proj split-K operation requires atomics and zeroing since it's the only place that needs initialization, while the down-GEMV and context updates can reuse preloaded values to avoid extra zeroing overhead. Defining MoE and MLA scratch buffers for each layer—router outputs, expert gate/up projections, and the q/kv projection tiles with a generic GEMV scheduler that handles variable column counts across units. Now working through the attention mechanism: RoPE-applied query and key tensors, attention score matrices with chunked partials for numerical stability, and the context output buffer that needs zeroing before the second pass phase to avoid stale data. Setting up the cache structure with separate buffers for key-value pairs and RoPE components, allocating space dynamically based on sequence length plus a margin for new tokens. So I can move the hattn preload into phase A for consistency across all layers, using dedicated copy units that read from x_i without conflicts. For layer 0, I'll keep phase Z handling x0 initialization and cache setup, then let phase A handle the hattn0 preload uniformly with other layers. The x_next preload can happen in phase D during the router phase when those CTAs are idle, after the phase C barrier ensures hattn_i is finalized. Now mapping out the actual phase sequence: phase 0 is Z for initialization, then each KDA layer i gets phases starting at 1+6i. Phase A runs the GEMV for qkvg across 128 units (4 matrices with 32 tiles each), adds beta computation as part of the unit list, and includes the hattn copy units—all preceded by rmsnorm preparation in shared memory. Phase B follows with the convolution update and S matrix handling, where only the unit with jb==0 writes the convolution windows. Phase C handles the output projection GEMV with 2304 dimensions split into 18 tiles across 4 K-chunks (each chunk is 1024 rows from the full 4096), producing 72 units that atomically add results into hattn, with fp32 to half conversion and prescaling in shared memory. Phase D starts with a router unit that reads the final hattn, applies rmsnorm for the MoE, computes logits and softmax for top-8 selection, then additional units copy the next layer's input. Phase E runs 9 expert slots with gate and up GEMVs across 144 units total, loading the MoE-selected input from global to shared memory, then Phase F handles the down GEMVs with 9 slots and 18 tiles per slot, using 2 K-chunks of 512 rows each, totaling 324 units across roughly 1.7 waves. For the down phase, I'm reconsidering whether to use KSPLIT=1 instead, which would give 162 units in a single wave—this avoids the multi-wave overhead, but since all 9 slots write to the same output columns, atomic operations are necessary regardless of the split strategy to handle the collisions. I'll stick with KSPLIT=1 and use atomicAdd for the partial results, preparing the activated hidden states (silu-gated up projection) per unit inside the loop since each unit processes a different slot. Now moving into layer 3's MLA phases: the attention projection splits into q_proj (48 tiles from 6144 dimensions) and kv_a (5 units handling 576 dimensions), plus copying the attention input from x3 into shared memory. The first unit applies RoPE to the queries and keys, reading from the rope lookup tables and writing the rotated embeddings, while the remaining units handle the absolute position embeddings across the query-key pairs. For the scoring phase, I need to determine the chunk size adaptively based on sequence length to balance memory and compute efficiency. With CHUNK=64, I get reasonable parallelism across different context lengths—33 units at 2K tokens and 257 at 16K—which keeps memory bandwidth manageable. Each processing unit then iterates through its chunk of positions, reading the key-value and rope rows for all 32 heads. For the dot product with query absolute values, I'm loading both qabs and qrope into shared memory once per CTA to avoid repeated L2 hits. That's about 64KB for qabs in fp32 plus 8KB for qrope, totaling 72KB—well within the 100KB dynamic shared memory limit on SM_90. I'm keeping qabs in fp32 rather than converting to half precision because the softmax is sensitive to score precision; even though half has enough bits for typical score magnitudes (1-10 range), the accumulated error from 512 terms could shift attention weights by around 1%, which is noticeable for cosine similarity. For the actual compute kernel, I'm organizing threads so each warp handles one head across the 512 sequence dimension, which gives 16K FLOPs per layer—manageable within the warp's throughput. But the shuffle-heavy reduction across 32 heads is expensive, so I'm reconsidering: assigning one thread per (layer, head) pair means 512 threads per block can each read the full 512-token key-value row from shared memory, though that creates a 64KB bottleneck that conflicts with the 72KB query allocation. Storing qabs transposed in shared memory avoids bank conflicts when lanes access it sequentially, while ckv broadcasts efficiently from global memory through L1 cache — each warp reads the same row once across 512 iterations, which is bandwidth-efficient despite the scalar load pattern. For a 64-l chunk per block, this keeps the computation at 512 MACs per lane in fp32. Now I'm computing per-head statistics: after each thread calculates its score, I reduce across lanes in shared memory to find the max and sum of exponentials per head, then write those partials to global memory as fp32 values. The context pass uses the same chunking strategy, reading back all the max and sum partials to compute final normalization factors. For the attention output, I'm organizing the computation so each warp handles one sequence position, reloading scores and computing softmax probabilities, then accumulating the weighted context vectors into shared memory—each thread multiplies its probability by the corresponding key-value element and adds it to the accumulator. Each thread holds a full column of context values across all heads in registers, reading the packed key-value row once per l with coalesced memory access, then performing 32 multiply-accumulates where the attention weights broadcast from shared memory — this avoids redundant reads and keeps atomics manageable at the end. The attention weights themselves are precomputed in a first pass by all threads working on the 2048 (l, h) pairs, stored in shared memory, then the main loop proceeds with synchronized access. Now stepping through the remaining projection and gating layers: the output projection uses 18 tiles across 4 KSPLIT chunks for 72 units total, atomically adding results back to the attention output, followed by the router computation on a single unit plus three copy operations, then the gate and up layers spanning 144 units, the down layer with 162 units and four atomic additions, and finally a tail phase casting the output to bfloat16 across 4 units — all coordinated through 28 synchronization barriers across 29 total phases. For the GEMV kernel handling int4 weights, I'm working with a column-major-packed weight matrix where each row corresponds to a K/2 slice and has stride N bytes, and each unit processes a column tile of 128 elements across aligned k-ranges, with the 512-thread CTA divided into 16 warps that each handle a sub-range of the k-dimension. Extracting nibbles from the packed uint32 to reconstruct half2 values for each column pair, then using those to compute fused multiply-add operations across the accumulator. 8 MACs per 4 bytes. For group boundaries every 64 packed rows, I need to fold in the scale and zero point: y02 += s2_{col0,col2} ⊙ (acc02 − z2 ⊙ Xg), where Xg is the sum of all x values in the group computed once per group per warp. Rather than have each lane redundantly sum 128 values from shared memory, I'll precompute the 18 group sums during the prepare stage and store them in shared memory, then use those precomputed values during the main kernel. For accumulators, I'm storing y02 and y13 as half2 pairs per group, converting them to fp32 after each group and fusing with the scale and zero-point multiplies. The scales and zeros load coalesced as bf16×4 per group per lane. The main loop iterates over groups, zeroing accumulators, processing 64 rows, then folding—but I need to ensure warps are assigned to whole groups rather than arbitrary K slices so the group folding stays aligned. Now I'm wrestling with how to distribute work across the 16 warps. If I split groups across warps, an uneven split like 18 groups across 16 warps leaves half the warps idle at the tail. Alternatively, splitting by columns instead of K—say 16 warps handling 8 columns each—gives poor vectorization since each lane would load only 1 byte. A hybrid approach of tiling 128 columns with a 2×8 warp grid, where each warp handles 64 columns and multiple groups, could work if lanes map to 2 columns and use uint16 loads, but that halves the LDG throughput. The cleaner option is keeping the lane-to-4-columns mapping within the 128-column tile. Instead, I could have each warp handle a slice of rows within every group—say warp w processes rows [w×4, w×4+4) across all groups, giving each warp 4 rows from the 64-row groups. Since the fold operation is linear, each warp computes its partial sum over its row slice and combines it with the scaling factor immediately. The −szX term, which doesn't depend on rows, gets computed once and assigned to warp 0 only. After that, I need to reduce the per-warp results across all 16 warps using shared memory—storing 128 columns × 16 warps × 4 values as 8KB of fp32 data. Looking at the overhead: for each 4-row group slice, I'm loading s and z (8 bytes) and doing about 2 fp32 operations per column, which is roughly 12% overhead relative to the fold itself. Alternatively, I could assign each warp a contiguous slice of columns rounded to group boundaries—for K=2304 (18 groups) across 16 warps, that'd be 14 warps handling 1 group each and 2 warps handling 2 groups to balance the load. The strided approach is cleaner: each warp w handles packed rows where (r/4) mod 16 equals w within a group. Since each group has 64 packed rows, warp w gets rows [g×64 + w×4, +4), accessing 4 consecutive rows then jumping 64 rows—the access pattern coalesces well per row and L2 cache is fine. I could also use a row-strided variant where rows r ≡ w (mod 16), giving each warp rows {w, w+16, w+32, w+48} per group—same row count either way. For the smaller GEMV case with K=1024 (8 groups × 64 rows = 512 packed rows), dividing across 16 warps gives 32 rows per warp, or 8 rows per group per warp. Now checking compute bounds: with a 128-column tile and K=2304, I'm looking at roughly 147KB of data, about 15K operations per lane, and across all threads that's around 480K SASS operations total. At roughly 1 operation per thread per cycle with instruction-level parallelism, that's about 936 cycles—roughly 1 microsecond at 2.6GHz. Memory bandwidth at 40GB/s per SM means 147KB transfers in about 3 microseconds. For the full phase A across 128 units, the aggregate picture shows 18.9MB total data spread across 128 SMs, which hits DRAM bandwidth limits at around 1.8TB/s rather than the per-SM 40GB/s—so the system becomes DRAM-bound at roughly 10.5 microseconds. That's acceptable since we have enough outstanding memory requests to keep the pipeline fed. Now looking at the full KDA layer breakdown: the Z phase takes 2µs, then each of the three main blocks cycles through phases A through F with varying costs—the MoE gate and up-projection alone consume 12µs at DRAM bandwidth, the down-projection takes 7µs, and accounting for synchronization barriers across the three blocks brings the total to around 156µs. The MLA path is more efficient, starting at 4.5µs for its first phase. Speedup looks like 10-14x compared to baseline. I should explore further optimizations like merging barrier phases across layers and using vectorized loads per lane. For the bf16 GEMV operations with beta and router, I'm keeping those as simple fp32 dot products since they're minimal overhead. On precision, I need to be careful with the prescaling—folding the factor of 8 into the group sum keeps accumulation safe, and the negative term involving X_g also derives from the prescaled values so it stays within bounds. The reference computation uses bf16 for xn with minimal rounding error, and I'm being careful with the RMSNorm mean over the full 2304 elements and epsilon at 1e-6. For phase E, I'm deciding to store xn_moe globally as plain half (unscaled) and handle the scaling consistently by treating all GEMV input x in shared memory as half x/8, recomputing group sums from those scaled values per CTA. Phase F prepare is next. For the h computation, I'm applying silu in fp32 on the gate and up matmul results before converting to half/8 for shared memory, which matches the reference approach. Phase C prepare handles the o_proj output similarly—casting o to bf16 before projection and storing as half/8 with group sums. For the MLA q_abs GEMV, I'm checking precision: the reference casts q to bf16 from q_proj output, then converts back to float for q_nope, so I need to match that bf16 intermediate value rather than keeping q6144 in fp32. Now I'm working through the q_abs computation itself—summing quantized nibbles scaled by their dequantization factors across the k-part dimension, computed directly in fp32 per unit. The layout has 256 packed rows with 8192 columns total, where each head's k-part occupies 128 consecutive columns within that row. I'm realizing the dequantization needs to happen per-element since the scales and zeros vary along the column dimension—each position d has its own scale and zero value. So I can't fold the scaling outside the reduction; instead, I need to apply the scale and zero for each specific d when computing the dot product. For the actual kernel implementation, I'm loading the scales and zeros into L2 cache which stays hot across the group of 64 rows, then per lane I'm converting 4 bytes into 8 dequantized values by subtracting the zero and multiplying by the scale for each d. The warp then reduces these partial sums across the 4 d values to accumulate the final result for both the even and odd c positions in that row. I'm also folding the attention scale directly into the quantized values in shared memory so the absolute values come out pre-scaled, and applying the rope rotation with the scale factor in the first unit. For the thread organization, I'm mapping warps to sequential batches of 16 sequence positions and lanes to heads, though I need to reconsider how to handle the 32 heads cleanly with this layout. The naive approach of loading ckv per iteration is way too slow—512 LDG instructions per lane across 16 warps balloons to 50-100K cycles. Instead, I should stage the entire ckv chunk into shared memory as half precision, then compute the score matrix as a small matrix multiplication between ckv and qabs. Now I'm laying out the computation: 512 threads organized as 16 lanes × 32 heads, processing 4 batches. Each thread reads from shared memory—ckv broadcasted across threads in the same lane, qabs laid out for lane-consecutive access—and uses half2 to process two columns at once, cutting the load instructions in half. At context length 2048 with 188 SMs, I'm looking at roughly 5 microseconds per unit, which scales to 10 microseconds for the full 16k case across two waves—but the instruction-level parallelism is bottlenecking performance. I'm exploring a better approach: reorganizing threads to pair h-dimensions together, so each thread handles multiple h-pairs while processing all 512 c values, using half2 accumulation to reduce memory pressure and improve throughput per instruction. Now I'm concerned about numerical precision with half2 accumulation—individual weight noise could compound, but if I split the 512 channels into smaller segments and accumulate each in half2 before promoting to fp32, the error stays manageable. The output error from weight noise would be around 1-2%, which preserves cosine similarity well enough. Rather than over-engineer this, I'll stick with fp32 accumulation while loading pairs of channels as half2 to keep memory bandwidth efficient. For the actual kernel, I'm weighing whether to broadcast and unpack half2 values to float2 for each operation—that's expensive in terms of instruction count and IPC. Instead, I'll use a half2-based approach where threads handle segments of the 512 channels, with each warp covering multiple segments across passes. The key is minimizing loads and shuffles: load the channel-key-value pair as half2, splat it across threads without extra shuffles, compute the absolute value in half2, then do the fused multiply-add. That brings the instruction count down to around 3-4 operations per channel pair. Scaling this up: 512 threads per batch doing 512 channels each means roughly 1800 instructions per warp, and with 32 warps handling the full unit of 2048 scores, I'm looking at about 57K total instructions—same ballpark as before, so I'm still hitting the IPC bottleneck around 5 microseconds per unit. I'm now considering whether tensor cores (wmma or mma instructions) could reduce the instruction count further by computing the attention scores directly as a matrix multiply of the channel-key-value matrix against the query absolute values. The tensor core approach would be much faster—maybe 2 microseconds including staging—but it adds complexity to the PTX code. For now I'll stick with the LDS and half-precision floating-point path since it's simpler and profiling can guide optimization later if needed. I'm also exploring whether I could reformulate the scores and context computation as a series of GEMVs instead, treating each head as a separate matrix-vector multiply, but that would require re-reading the matrix 32 times, which is inefficient. The staged shared memory approach reads the context matrix exactly once, which is good. For the context pass itself, each thread handles a fixed column and reads across rows with stride-1 coalescing, then performs the multiply-accumulate operations—roughly 4200 instructions per thread across 16 warps. Using half-precision pairs over the head dimension cuts this down to about 2100 instructions per thread, dropping the latency to around 3 microseconds. The precision is acceptable since the attention values stay small (p ≤ 1, context vectors ~0.1), and I convert back to fp32 for the atomic accumulation at the end. For the full MLA attention at 16k tokens, the scoring phase takes about 7 microseconds and the context phase adds another 4.5 microseconds, but the 18 microseconds of DRAM traffic overlaps with computation, so realistically I'm looking at around 25 microseconds total. Now moving into the KDA phase B for the state update. Figuring out the memory layout for loading S values—threads need to access S[h][i][j] efficiently, and I'm realizing the stride pattern doesn't coalesce well with the current indexing scheme, so I'm reconsidering how to map threads to memory addresses to get proper coalescing across the warp. Now I'm verifying the output tensor layout—making sure the reshaped o tensor with dimensions [H*128] maps correctly to the o_proj input, and confirming the convolution window stores pre-activation values before concatenating with previous states. For the decay computation, I'm verifying that g gets computed as negative softplus of the raw values, which gives us the sigmoid form we need — the reference uses bf16 projection output cast to float, while we're working with unrounded fp32 values. For beta, I'm checking the sigmoid applied to beta_out, where the reference does a bf16 linear layer that accumulates internally in fp32 before casting back to float for the sigmoid. Now I'm looking at the Phase Z cache copy — a 16MB transfer on the first step using all available CTAs with vectorized memcopy, with the flag length passed as an argument. For the router unit, I'm computing the attention hidden state through RMSnorm in shared memory across 512 threads, then writing the normalized values as half precision to global memory. The logits computation uses thread groups to sum 288 terms each from the shared memory fp32 values multiplied by the router weights in bf16, where the weight matrix is laid out in row-major order. For the softmax over 64 experts, I'm finding the max and computing exponentials in fp32, then using a single warp to extract the top-8 experts through iterative argmax-and-mask operations. The weights are normalized by their sum with a small epsilon, scaled by 2.446, and I'm storing the 8 expert IDs and weights plus a shared expert slot with weight 1.0. To match PyTorch's tie-breaking behavior, I'm using strict greater-than comparisons with index preference so lower indices win on ties. I need to be careful about the router input — it should be the RMSnorm of the hidden state. Now I'm realizing there's a precision risk: the router input is in BF16, and even tiny differences from fp32 computation could flip which expert lands in the top-8 boundary, potentially changing the output significantly. With 64 near-uniform probabilities around 0.015 each, the gaps between adjacent experts are typically 1e-3 to 1e-2 relative, so the probability of a flip per router call is roughly 1 in 1e3 to 1e4. Across 6 test cases with 4 layers, that's 24 router calls total, so I should benchmark the gate shapes and check whether a flipped expert choice actually causes a noticeable output difference. If an expert does flip, it changes about 1/9 of that layer's MoE output, which might drop the final cosine similarity to around 0.99—still likely within the 0.98 threshold for the output, S, and c_kv checks. To minimize this risk, I'll mimic BF16 precision by rounding the router input to BF16 before the dot product, then accumulating in fp32 for the linear layer, which cuBLAS handles correctly. This means casting the fp32 values to BF16 and back in-register when computing the router logits—a cheap operation. The remaining source of difference would be the summation order. The ordering difference versus cuBLAS is around 1e-6 relative error, so the flip risk from exact ties is negligible. The MLA softmax over a large sequence length is fine since there are no discrete choices there—only the top-8 selection poses a discrete hazard. For the down-projection weighting, the shared expert weight is applied correctly, and the nondeterministic order of atomic adds during accumulation is acceptable. Each output element gets 9 atomic operations per slot, which is fine. The final step casts the accumulated values back to BF16 before writing to the output buffer. I need to make sure the framework label is set to "cuda_raw" for check.py, and the solution shouldn't import Triton. Now for the solution structure: I'm setting up imports for torch, the CUDA source string (around 1200 lines), and module classes that mirror the state_dict names for compatibility. The Model class builds the same structure as the reference, and since check.py loads the state_dict after construction, I don't need to worry about random initialization—the loaded weights will overwrite whatever's there. The kernel will receive pointers to the loaded tensor buffers directly. For the pointer management, I'm building a struct with around 190 pointers that gets constructed once at setup time and cached. On each step, only the per-step pointers and flags update. Since weights could be replaced by another load_state_dict call, I'm using a lazy setup approach: the first step() call triggers setup, and if any tensor's data pointer changes, setup runs again. This keeps the overhead minimal while ensuring the kernel always has valid pointers. In the step path, I'm passing either user-provided pointers for conv windows and a cache copy flag, or falling back to our previous ping buffers. The kernel updates the state tensors in-place—the cache views get reassigned, S gets modified directly in the user's tensor, and we return the output buffer and updated state. One thing to watch: the output buffer gets reused across steps, so I need to make sure it's only consumed once per iteration in the benchmark loop. For the first-step detection, I'm tracking the data pointers and cache key-value lengths we've handed out to distinguish between continuing an existing state versus a fresh one. When a new state comes in, I check if its cache pointer and length match what we expect — if they do, we're continuing the same conversation; if not, we need to copy the caches and read the user's conversation pointers fresh. I'm also managing the ping-pong buffer index for alternating cache writes, and when initializing a fresh state, I'm allocating the cache with some headroom beyond the current sequence length. For the C++ and CUDA implementation, I'm wrapping everything in a single compiled module with a setup function that takes all the weight tensors (passed as a vector to keep the signature clean and generated programmatically) and a step function that processes one token at a time with the hidden state, output buffers, per-step tensors, sequence length, and control flags. I'm using cooperative kernel launches with a struct to pass arguments, and I'll verify the device supports cooperative launches before attempting it. Tuning occupancy with 512 threads... Checking if 512 threads per CTA can saturate DRAM bandwidth — with 8 outstanding loads per thread and an unroll factor of 8 rows, that's roughly 16KB in flight per SM with ~600 cycle latency, so I need to verify occupancy constraints given the 72KB shared memory limit on this GPU. For the GEMV kernel, I'm considering whether to use wider loads (uint2/uint4 for 8 or 16 columns per lane) versus sticking with uint32 for 4 columns, which maintains clean 128B sector utilization per warp. The tile size of 128 keeps things simple, so uint32 loads should be fine. One more edge case to handle: when N isn't a multiple of 128 — like the 576-column case that breaks into 4 full tiles plus a 64-column remainder, or the separate bf16 path for beta with N=32. For partial tiles, I'll mask out lanes whose column index exceeds the actual column count, using predicates to skip loads and stores for those lanes. For the bf16 path specifically, beta's weight matrix is [32, 2304] in row-major order, so the reduction happens along the contiguous dimension for each output row — straightforward to implement. Now I'm working through the unit allocation: a single "BETA" unit with 512 threads handles 32 outputs, with each warp responsible for 2 outputs and performing a strided dot product across 2304 elements. Each warp reads about 9.2KB, totaling 147KB per unit, which should fit in L2 and execute in a couple microseconds. In phase A with 32 tiles of qkvg operations, I'm fitting 128 GEMV units plus the beta unit and 4 copy units into 133 total units across 188 CTAs in a single wave. The router weight is also row-major at [64, 2304]. Now I'm setting up the implementation: I'll write solution.py with the module classes, weight table, and CUDA kernels, plus a test_stages.py to verify against reference intermediates. The debug path stays in scratch files while keeping solution.py clean—the import checker ensures scratch files can reference the harness without contaminating the solution. Writing the solution structure... Defining the weight tensor layout for each KDA layer, specifying the exact dimensions and data types for attention norms, quantized projections, and convolution weights, then doing the same for the MLA block with its own projection and key-value configurations. Continuing to map out the scratch buffer allocations—tracking which tensors get reused across layers and phases to minimize memory overhead, with careful attention to when each buffer can be safely overwritten by the next operation. Accumulating the rope-based query-key products by looping over the 64 dimensions with broadcasted key values and storing the query rope matrix as [r][h] in half precision. Then setting up the scoring buffers—a float32 scores matrix capped at sequence length, separate max and sum reduction buffers, a context tensor for the value projections, output accumulator, final output in bfloat16, and separate capacity buffers for the cached keys and key-rope values. Now I'm planning the convolution window ping-pong buffers across the three layers with their different kinds, and determining the capacity allocation—starting with 17408 to handle the maximum shape, which gives about 20MB of memory. The number of channels gets computed from the sequence length at kernel launch time, and I'm structuring the kernel phases as straight-line code without branching: first the initial phase, then looping through the three layers with their KDA operations and internal syncs, followed by the MLA layer. For the KDA phase, I'm dividing the 133 units across GEMV operations for query, key, value, and gate matrices (units 0–127 handling different matrix types and column tiles), a beta unit, and then copy units for the attention intermediate results. The GEMV writes 32-bit floats to the output buffer. Phase B and C then process the attention computation with different unit-to-tile mappings and chunk assignments. Continuing with the MLA A phase where units handle the projection operations and copy operations for the attention mechanism. Now I'm setting up MLA B where unit 0 handles rope and append operations while the remaining units process the query absolute values row by row. The key constraint is that shared memory preparation for the query values happens once per CTA before the main loop, with units distributed across CTAs such that CTA 68 gets both unit 68 and the rope unit 256. Then MLA C handles the NCH units for the channel dimension. For the next stage, I'm loading the query absolute values into shared memory with a specific layout pattern to avoid bank conflicts—the threads cooperatively copy data using 128-bit loads, then process 32-row batches. After computing the attention scores across the 512 threads with HFMA2 operations and rope embeddings, I'm converting the partial half-precision results to full precision, then performing block-level reductions to find the max and sum of exponentials for each head. The scores get written to global memory while I simultaneously reduce across the 64 query positions for each head dimension using shared memory as a tree structure. Now I'm handling the second phase where each warp serially processes the NCH channels (up to 273) to combine the per-block max and sum values, computing the final normalized statistics that get written back to global memory for each channel and head. Refining the per-thread accumulation strategy: loading bf16 key-value pairs and converting them to half precision for the HFMA operations, then broadcasting the softmax probabilities from shared memory across all threads in the unit. The loop over 64 sequence positions with conversion overhead and fused multiply-add operations comes to roughly 2300 instructions per thread, translating to about 3 microseconds per unit. Considering an alternative with fp32 accumulators instead... Actually, keeping half2 precision is better—the alternative with 32 fp32 accumulators and strided atomics would be slower. After the multiply-accumulate loop, each thread converts its 16 half2 results to 32 fp32 values and atomically updates the context buffer, with threads handling consecutive columns naturally coalescing their atomic writes. For the MLA expansion phase, I'm setting up a GEMV operation across 32 units where each unit processes a 512-element context vector against a 128-column weight tile, with the weight columns offset appropriately within the 8192-column matrix. Setting up the copy loop with stride-based access across all CTAs to move cache and key-rope data into the appropriate buffers, then coordinating which phases read and write which layer inputs with proper synchronization. Now I'm working through the convolution window buffering strategy — Python will pass read and write pointers for each layer at each step, but I'm simplifying by using a rotating window buffer that alternates between two sets of storage, with views into the written buffer exposed back to the user as the state tensors for the next step. For RoPE positions, I'm computing the inverse frequencies using the standard formula with double precision for accuracy, then in the kernel I'm applying those to get the rotation angles — sinf and cosf handle the full range up to 16384 positions reliably through CUDA's payne-hanek reduction, which is fine even though it's slower since we're only computing across 32 lanes. For the actual rotation, I'm interleaving the even and odd components using the standard RoPE formula, applying the MLA scale at the write stage for the query but not the key to avoid double-application. The reference implementation stores the rope-transformed key as bf16 in the cache, which matches what we're doing, while the query goes through f32 rope computation before being cast to bf16 — we're keeping the query in f32 directly instead. Now checking the attention scores: they need to include all L+1 rows with the new token at index L, and the softmax denominators are computed across the sequence length dimension per head. The reference uses the cached k_rope after appending the new row and applies the attention over the full L+1 cache, which is what we're doing with the new row participating through the cache row at position L. For the output projection, the reference reshapes the output to H*v_head and indexes it as [h][e] where e spans 128 value dimensions — our o_buf layout matches this with o_buf[h*128+e], and the projection weight matrix dimensions and indexing align correctly. Checking the channel mapping for the convolution: the q_proj output column n maps to h*128 + dk, which corresponds to the same column index in the convolution weights, and the window dimensions are consistent. Verifying the GEMV Y scaling formula with the group-wise computation and precomputed X_g values, then checking the expert GEMV pointers with their memory offsets, and finally the beta unit computation where each output element is a weighted sum across the input dimensions. Now confirming that beta operates on the post-norm attention input (the RMSnorm'd xn), which is stored in shared memory at half precision divided by 8, and we accumulate in fp32 before applying sigmoid — either here or deferring to phase B depending on what saves work. There's a precision subtlety with the router: xn_moe is stored as half for the GEMVs, but the reference input uses bf16 rounding, which produces different results than half rounding. To match the reference as closely as possible, the router unit itself converts from f32 to bf16 within its own shared memory, even though the expert GEMVs receive half-precision inputs — this slight mismatch in expert outputs is acceptable since there's no discrete risk downstream. For the KDA convolution, the reference uses bf16 for window rows and values with f32 computation, whereas we're using bf16 window rows but f32 values without rounding, which introduces a small deviation. The core plan is solid: I need to round the convolution values to bf16 during compute to match the reference more closely, which is straightforward since the window is already stored in bf16. The group-level adjustment then applies z_eff = z + 1024 against the accumulated sum A_g = Σ x·(1024+nib) across the warp's 4-row slice. For |x| ≤ 30, the accumulation stays safely within half-precision range. Now checking whether RMSNorm-ed activations stay within bounds: theoretical max is around 48 (single spike scaled by norm weight), and since the norm weights initialize to ones and never get randomized, they stay at 1.0. With |x| ≤ 48 and the per-element scaling of x/8 in each FMA, I need to verify the final accumulation doesn't overflow. Computing the worst case: each of 8 terms contributes up to (48/8)·1039 ≈ 6234, totaling around 50K—dangerously close to the float16 limit of 65504. But the real risk is the MoE down-path where intermediate products from silu·up could push |x| much higher (potentially 100+), which would cause overflow. Switching to HSUB2 with values in [0,15] dramatically reduces per-term contributions to 1.9·|x|, allowing |x| up to 4000 safely before overflow becomes a concern. I'm writing a half2 extraction function that unpacks 4-bit fields from a packed uint32, applies a bias offset, and subtracts 1024 to center the values—using explicit intrinsics since torch disables operator overloads. I also need to handle masking for cases where the column count is less than 128, checking lane conditions to avoid out-of-bounds access on the tail portion of the key-value array. For the reduction phase, I'm setting up a shared memory buffer to collect partial sums from each warp, then synchronizing across the entire thread block to perform a cross-warp reduction where threads accumulate values and write the final results back to global memory with proper bounds checking. Different units in the same phase can write to different output pointers depending on whether atomic operations are needed, which gets parametrized at the call site. Avoids projecting whole cache through kv_b. - Weight traffic budget ≈ 220MB/step int4 → ~0.12ms floor at 1.8TB/s. Baseline measured 7.7-9.3 ms/tok → target speedup ~20-40x. - Single cooperative CUDA kernel: cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel, grid=188 CTAs × 512 threads, cooperative_groups grid.sync() barriers (~1.03µs each measured), ~29 phases/barriers per step, dynamic smem ~72-96KB (requires cudaFuncSetAttribute(cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize); opt-in max 101376 B/block; 1 CTA/SM at that smem). - int4→fp16 magic-number dequant (AWQ/Marlin trick): per uint32 (4 columns × 2 k): `(w & 0x000F000F) | 0x64006400` → half2 (1024+nib) for cols {0,2} at even k; `(w>>8)&0x000F000F` → cols {1,3}; `>>4`, `>>12` variants for odd k. conv recomputed redundantly per (head, j-block) state unit; window written only by jb==0 unit. - Precision decisions: half2 group accumulation → fp32 across groups (cosine 0.98 is loose); router computed from bf16-rounded xn to minimize top-8 flip risk vs reference (the only discrete hazard); conv val rounded to bf16 to match reference; sinf/cosf (not __sinf) for RoPE at pos up to ~16900; KDA q-scale folded into conv output; MLA scale folded into q_abs and rope'd q_rope (not k). - Python-side per step: one extension call → one cooperative launch; view-only state updates (st[3]["c_kv"] = ckv_cap[:L+1], st[i]["cq"] = ping-pong view); S updated in-place in user tensor; first-step detection via data_ptr tracking → in-kernel cache copy (flag arg) in phase Z; out buffer reused (input read in phase Z, output written in tail phase — safe with same pointer). - Lazy setup after load_state_dict (load copies in-place so pointers stable; .to(device) changes pointers → setup on first step + cheap per-step pointer guard); weights passed as std::vector<torch::Tensor> in fixed order → C++ caches ~190 raw pointers in a kernel-arg struct (<4KB param limit OK). - Phase schedule (~29 phases): Z (copy hidden→x0 fp32, zero ctx, optional cache copy); per KDA layer i∈{0,1,2}: A (133 units: 128 qkvg GEMV tiles + 1 beta unit + 4 hattn-preload copies; prepare = redundant rmsnorm→smem half/8 + group sums), B (128 units (h, jb=32 j-cols): redundant conv+silu, decay=sigmoid(−g), S column update with pred reduction, o write; jb==0 writes new conv windows), C (72 o_proj units atomicAdd→hattn), D (unit 0 = router: rmsnorm(hattn,moe_norm)→xn_moe global half + bf16-rounded logits + softmax + top-8 + weight norm ×2.446 → ids[9]/wts[9]; units 1-4 copy x_{i+1}←hattn), E (144 gate/up units: 9 slots × 2 × 8 tiles, K=2304 N=1024, → hg/hu fp32 exclusive), F (162 down units: 9 slots × 18 tiles, K=1024; prepare = silu(hg)·hu → smem half/8; atomicAdd wts[slot]·y → x_{i+1}). MLA (layer 3): A (48 q_proj tiles + 5 kv_a units (last tile 64 cols) + 4 hattn3 copies), B (unit 0: RoPE q/k + append ckv/krope cap rows at L + write qr[64][32] half; units 1-256: q_abs per packed row c2, per-element dequant with per-column s/z, output qabs[c][h] half global), C (scores: NCH=⌈(L+1)/64⌉ chunk units; prepare loads qabs 32KB + qr 4KB smem; stage ckv 32 rows→smem half; threads (l, h2-pair) HFMA2 dot 512c + 64 rope; per-chunk mx/sm[NCH][32] + raw scores fp32 global), D (ctx pass: same chunks; prepare = redundant global M[h]/S[h] from mx/sm; stage p[l][h] smem; thread↔c with 16 half2 h-pair accumulators over 64 l; atomicAdd → ctx[32][512] fp32), E (32 Wv units: per-head GEMV K=512 N=128, x=ctx[h], col offset 256h+128 → o_buf), F (72 o_proj units → hattn3), G (router + copy x4), H (gate/up), I (down → x4), Tail (cast x4 → out bf16). - Scratch tensors: x[5][2304] f32, hattn[4][2304] f32, qkvg[4×4096] f32 (reused across KDA layers, exclusive writes), betaout[32] f32 (sigmoid pre-applied), o_buf[4096] f32, xn_moe[2304] half, ids[16] i32 + wts[16] f32, hg/hu[9×1024] f32, q6144 f32, kva[576] f32, qabs[512][32] half, qr[64][32] half, scores[LCAP][32] f32, mx/sm[NCHMAX][32] f32, ctx[32][512] f32, out[2304] bf16, ckv_cap[LCAP][512] bf16 + kr_cap[LCAP][64] bf16 (LCAP ≈ 17408), conv window ping-pong tensor [3][2][3][3][4096] bf16. - Estimated solution latency ~215-250µs/tok → expected speedup ~30-40x vs measured baseline. 3. Key Technical Concepts: - Reference math (verified from full reference.py read this turn): - QuantLinear.forward: `(x.float() @ dequant_weight.float()).to(bf16)` — fp32 accumulate, bf16-rounded output. - KDA.step: q/k/v/g = QuantLinear(2304→4096). `_short_conv`: `win = cat([prev(3,C), val[None]])`, out = Σ_t win[t]·conv_w[idx].T (fp32), SiLU, `.to(bf16)`; returns new window `win[1:]` (stores PRE-activation bf16 values). `q = conv_q.view(32,128).float() * 128**-0.5`; `g = -softplus(g_proj(x).float())` so decay = exp(g) = sigmoid(−g_raw); `beta = sigmoid(beta_proj(x).float())` (beta_proj nn.Linear(2304→32) bf16). State update: `S = st["S"]·exp(g)[:,:,None]; pred = (S·k[:,:,None]).sum(1); S += beta[:,None,None]·k[:,:,None]·(v−pred)[:,None,:]; o = (S·q[:,:,None]).sum(1)`; o → bf16 → o_proj(4096→2304). S index convention: S[h][i][j], i = key dim, j = value dim. - MLA.step: pos = `st["c_kv"].shape[0]` BEFORE append; q_proj(2304→32×192) [128 nope | 64 rope per head]; kv_a(2304→576) [512 c_kv | 64 k_rope], NO norm on c_kv; RoPE interleaved even/odd, theta 10000, `inv = theta^(−2r/64)`; cache append via torch.cat (bf16); kv_b(512→32×256) [128 k_nope | 128 v per head]; scores = (q_nope·k_nope + q_rope·k_rope)·192^-0.5; softmax over L; o = Σ p·v (fp32) → bf16 → o_proj(4096→2304). Note kvb output is bf16-rounded per QuantLinear before .float(). - MoE.step: probs = softmax(router(x).float()); `w, idx = topk(probs, 8)`; `w = w/(w.sum()+1e-9)*2.446`; out = Σ w_j·ffn_j(x) + shared ffn (unweighted); ffn: `h = silu(x.f@Wg)·(x.f@Wu)` fp32 (no bf16 round on h) then `h @ Wd`. Router input = rmsnorm(h, moe_norm) in bf16. - Block.step: `h = x + attn.step(rmsnorm(x, attn_norm), st); return h + moe.step(rmsnorm(h, moe_norm))` — residuals in bf16. - init_state: KDA {S fp32 (32,128,128), cq/ck/cv bf16 (3,4096)}; MLA {c_kv bf16 (ctx,512), k_rope bf16 (ctx,64)}; attn_norm/moe_norm initialized to ones but loaded from state_dict. - MLA absorption: q_abs[c,h] = Σ_d q_nope[h,d]·W_kvb[c, 256h+d] (192^-0.5 scale folded in); scores from cached c_kv·q_abs + k_rope·q_rope; ctx[h,c] = Σ_l p[l,h]·c_kv[l,c]; o[h,e] = Σ_c ctx[h,c]·W_kvb[c, 256h+128+e]. Avoids projecting the cache through kv_b. - Single cooperative CUDA kernel: cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel, grid = 188 CTAs × 512 threads, cooperative_groups grid.sync() (~1.03µs each, measured), ~29 barriers/step ≈ 30µs overhead, dynamic smem request 80KB (requires `cudaFuncSetAttribute(cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize)`; opt-in max measured 101376 B; 1 CTA/SM at that size). - int4→half magic dequant: per uint32 (4 cols c0..c3 × lo/hi nibbles): `(w & 0x000F000F) | 0x64006400` → half2 = (1024+n) for cols (c0,c2) even-k; `(w>>8)&...` → (c1,c3); `>>4`/`>>12` for odd-k. DECISION: subtract 1024 immediately via `__hsub2` (NOT offset-folding into zeros — overflow analysis: offset-fold accumulates ~1039·|x| terms and overflows half for |x|>~60, while HSUB2 keeps per-term ≤15·|x/8|, safe to |x|~4000). Group fold: y_warp += s·A_warp, and z-term `y −= s·z·gsum[g]` applied by WARP 0 ONLY (all warps subtracting full-group gsum would over-subtract 16× — bug caught in design). xs stored in smem as half PRE-SCALED by 1/8; gsum[g] = per-group sum of xs. - GEMV unit: (col-tile 128, k-chunk). CTA 512 threads = 16 warps; lane ↔ 4 consecutive cols (colbase = col0 + lane*4, one uint32 load per packed row); warp w handles packed rows [g*64 + w*4, g*64 + w*4 + 4) per group g (64 packed rows = 128 k per group, 16 warps × 4 rows covers exactly); per-lane accumulators a02 (cols c0,c2) and a13 (cols c1,c3) as half2 per group, folded to fp32 y[4] with scales/zeros loaded as 4 consecutive bf16 (8B aligned — all N values 576/1024/2304/4096/6144/8192 divisible by 4, so uint32/8B alignment holds everywhere). Cross-warp reduce via smem ysum[16][128] f32, thread t<128 sums 16, applies wmul, store or atomicAdd to `out[out_base + i]`. Masked lanes (ncols=64 for kv_a tail): guard loads/stores only, never syncthreads. Two __syncthreads per gemv call (before writing ysum, after reduce). - Split-K with fp32 atomicAdd into residual-preloaded accumulators (hattn preloaded with x by copy units in phase A; x_next preloaded with hattn in router phase) — avoids zeroing passes; only ctx[32][512] needs zeroing (phase Z). - Precision matching decisions (each mapped to a reference line): round conv val, conv outputs (before q scale), g_raw, beta logit, KDA o, MLA q6144, kva to bf16 where reference does; router path rounds h to bf16 before rmsnorm and rounds xn to bf16 for logits (minimizes top-8 flip risk — the only discrete hazard); no v materialization means kvb bf16 rounding is skipped (~2^-9 relative, fine at cosine 0.98); hattn/x kept fp32 instead of bf16 residual (accepted deviation); half2 segment accumulation (64-term segments) promoted to fp32 for scores; sinf/cosf/expf/powf accurate versions — NO `--use_fast_math` (RoPE at pos up to ~16900 breaks with __sinf). - Phase schedule with max_phase debug (`BARRIER()` = `grid.sync(); if (++ph >= max_phase) return;`): Z=1 (copy hidden→x0 f32 — must read hidden BEFORE tail since out buffer may alias input across steps; zero ctx; optional cache copy of L rows when fresh); per KDA layer i∈{0,1,2}: A (133 units: 128 qkvg GEMV tiles (mat=u>>5, tile=u&31, N=4096, 18 groups) + unit 128 beta (16 warps × 2 outputs, lane-strided dot over 2304, ×8 to undo xs prescale, sigmoid applied) + units 129-132 copy hattn[i]←x[i]; CTA prepare: rmsnorm(x[i], attn_norm)→xs+gsum), B (128 units (h=u>>2, jb=(u&3), j0=jb*32): threads t<384 (kind=t/128, c=t%128) redundant conv+silu→qc/kc/vc smem (q ×128^-0.5 after bf16 round); t∈[384,512): dec[c]=1/(1+expf(g_raw)); jb==0 unit writes new ping-pong windows (row0=old row1, row1=old row2, row2=bf16(val)); state threads t: j=t&31 (jcol=j0+j), ic=t>>5, 8 rows i=ic*8..+7: sreg[r]=S[(i0+r)*128+jcol]·dec[i0+r], predp+=sreg·kc; smem red[16][32] reduce → pred[j]; sreg += beta_h·kc·(vc[jcol]−pred); op += sreg·qc; store S back f32; reduce op → obuf[h*128+j0+j]), C (72 o_proj units: tile=u%18, kchunk=u/18, g0=kchunk*8, atomicAdd→hattn[i]; prepare: bf16-round obuf→xs), D router (block 0: smem xh=f32(bf16(hattn)) rmsnorm w/ moe_norm; write xn_moe global half unscaled; logits 64 outputs × 8 sub-lanes, bf16-rounded xh; thread-0 serial softmax + top-8 (strict > = first-index tiebreak) + wts=p/(Σ+1e-9)·2.446 → ids[8]/wts[8], slot 8 = shared expert wmul 1.0; blocks 1-4: copy x[i+1]←hattn[i]), E gate/up (144 units: slot=u/16, gu=(u/8)%2, tile=u%8; e=ids[slot] or shared; N=1024, 18 groups; →hg/hu[slot*1024+...] exclusive; prepare from xn_moe ×0.125), F down (162 units: slot=u/18, tile=u%18; per-unit prepare inside unit loop with syncthreads: xs[i]=half(silu(hg)·hu·0.125), 8 gsums; N=2304; atomicAdd wmul·y → x[i+1]). MLA layer 3: A=20 (48 q_proj tiles→q6144 f32, N=6144; units 48-52 kv_a (last tile 64 cols, N=576)→kva; 53-56 copy hattn[3]←x[3]), B=21 (257 units; CTA prepare: qn smem[32][128] f32 = f32(bf16(q6144[h*192+d]))·scale_mla (16KB); units u<256: qabs row c2=u, gc=c2>>6, warp w → heads 2w,2w+1, lane d0=lane*4 loads uint32 wq_kvb[c2*8192 + h*256 + d0], per-byte lo/hi nibbles with per-column s/z (scalar bf16 loads, L2-hot 128KB), warp shfl reduce → qabs[(2c2)*32+h], [(2c2+1)*32+h] half; unit 256: all 512 threads write ckv_cap[L*512+i]=bf16(kva[i]), then t<32: r=t rope: inv=powf(10000,−2r/64), ang=L·inv, rotate q (per h, write qr[(2r)*32+h], qr[(2r+1)*32+h] half ×scale_mla) and k_rope→kr_cap[L*64+2r,2r+1] with f32(bf16(kva)) inputs), scores=22 (NCH=⌈(L+1)/64⌉ units; CTA prepare: copy qabs_s 32KB + qr_s 4KB smem; per unit two 32-row batches: stage ckv_s[32][512] half (bf162→float2→half2) + kr_s[32][64]; compute threads t=lloc*16+h2 (32 l × 16 h-pairs): 8 segments × 64 c: splat(ckv_s[lloc][c]) HFMA2 with *(half2*)(qabs_s+c*32+h2*2), promote per segment to float2; + rope 64 r; write score2 → scores[l*32+h] global f32 AND red smem[64][32]; then t<32: per-h chunk max + sumexp → mx/sm[ch*32+h]; ~5-8µs total at 16k), ctx=23 (NCH units; CTA prepare parallel M/S: 16 parts × 32 h partial max/sumexp over chunks → M[h], Sinv[h] smem; stage p_s[64][32] half = expf(scores−M)·Sinv; main: thread=c (512), loop lloc<cnt: splat half(f32(ckv bf16)), 16 half2 accumulators over *(half2*)(p_s+lloc*32+2*h2); 32 atomicAdd → ctx[h*512+c]), wv=24 (32 units h: per-unit prepare xs=half(bf16(ctx[h])/8) K=512 4 groups; col0=h*256+128 in N=8192, out_base=obuf+h*128), oproj=25 (72 units → atomicAdd hattn[3]; prepare bf16-round obuf), router=26/gateup=27/down=28 (→x[4]), BARRIER→ph=29, tail (t<2304: out[i]=bf16(x4[i])). Normal run needs max_phase ≥ 30 (use 1000); max_phase=N runs exactly N phases for stage testing. - Kernel arg struct (single by-value param via `void* args[] = {&karg}`): Weights (~150 ptrs: attn_norm/moe_norm[4], kda_wq/sc/zr[3][5] (q,k,v,g,o), beta_w[3], conv_w[3] ([3][4096][4], kind stride 16384), mla_wq/sc/zr[4] (q, kv_a, kv_b, o), router[4], moe_wq/sc/zr[4][6] (gate,up,down,s_gate,s_up,s_down)) + Scratch ptrs + StepArgs (hidden, S[3], winr[3][3], ckv_src/kr_src, copy_len, L, max_phase) — total <1.6KB, fine. Looking at the Z phase timing, it's showing 21µs when it should be around 3µs for copying 2304 floats and zeroing 16K floats—the discrepancy likely comes from launch overhead and kernel teardown costs in the cooperative kernel, which adds roughly 10-20µs on top. The negative delta for kda0.A suggests timing noise from phase overlap and launch pipelining effects. The real puzzle is why early phases run fast at layer 0 but slow down dramatically at layers 1 and 2—kda1.A jumps to 10.6µs and kda2.A to 27.3µs despite doing identical work per layer. This points to L2 cache behavior: with 128MB L2 and a 214MB working set, earlier layers' weights get evicted as later layers are accessed, but since max_phase=k only touches the first k phases' weights, the cache dynamics should favor early phases staying resident rather than getting pushed out. Actually, the profiling method itself is distorted—at small k the working set fits entirely in L2, making everything fast, but as k grows past L2 capacity, adding each new phase causes evictions that contaminate the deltas for all phases. The true per-phase cost at full k=29 differs from what the incremental measurements suggest. The final 382µs is the ground truth, and it reveals the kernel is fundamentally L2-capacity-bound rather than bandwidth-bound. With 214MB weights against 128MB L2, roughly 86MB must come from DRAM, yet the observed latency suggests we're hitting occupancy or latency limits in the GEMV inner loops rather than saturating the 1.79TB/s DRAM bandwidth—phase-level measurements show only ~0.7TB/s utilization even when missing L2. The access pattern explains this: each CTA's 16 warps load 4 consecutive rows of 128 bytes per iteration, with warps covering the full 8KB contiguous region per group. Each warp reads 128B from a single row (32 lanes × 4 bytes per lane), and the CTA streams through K×128B slices with a specific stride pattern based on the row indexing. The bottleneck is memory-level parallelism. With only 4 outstanding loads per warp per iteration followed by a dependent HFMA chain, and just one CTA per SM with 16 warps, the total in-flight data per SM is around 8KB. To saturate the 1.79TB/s bandwidth across 188 SMs requires roughly 5.7KB in flight per SM, so we're barely meeting the requirement—the HFMA dependency chain is limiting throughput. I'm reconsidering the load strategy: instead of 4B per thread, I could load 8B or 16B per lane to reduce instruction overhead and improve efficiency. But I need to reconsider the data layout—the packed row is N bytes wide (since each byte holds two K-values for one column), so a 128-column tile spans 128 bytes. To boost memory-level parallelism, I'm thinking about unrolling more rows per warp iteration—maybe 8 packed rows instead of 4, so each warp issues 8 independent loads before the FMA chain starts. That would increase the in-flight data per lane from 16B to more. The challenge is restructuring the warp-to-row mapping: if each warp handles 8 rows and there are 16 warps, that's 128 rows per iteration, which means processing two groups per iteration instead of one. I'm considering a few approaches: one is having each warp process 4 rows from group g and 4 rows from group g+1 concurrently, which doubles the in-flight loads but requires separate accumulators since scales and zeros differ per group. Another option is software pipelining—unroll the group loop by 2 and prefetch the next group's data while computing the current one, though the compiler might already be doing that given the straightforward loop structure. The 0.7TB/s throughput suggests it's not, so there's room to improve here. I'm also thinking about cache behavior: the weights are streamed once per step but reused across steps, and with 128MB L2 versus 214MB working set, there's potential LRU thrashing. Using streaming cache hints like `__ldcs` for the largest matrices could help preserve L2 for smaller, hotter data. The real question is whether 60% of the weights could actually persist in L2 if the replacement policy were optimized. For immediate experiments, I want to unroll the g-loop by 2 in the gemv kernel to process two MLP groups at once, and measure the actual relaunch overhead separately—that 21µs at k=1 seems large and includes launch, pybind, and barrier costs, so isolating the pure grid.sync cost across the 29 phases would clarify how much is fixed overhead versus per-phase synchronization. I'm also considering whether distributing idle CTAs across phases with fewer units could help, since phases with <188 units leave SMs underutilized, though if we're bandwidth-bound at 0.7TB/s that might not be the limiting factor anyway. My plan is to split the phase A GEMVs into k-chunks to increase parallelism and keep all SMs busy while boosting memory-level parallelism, then apply similar chunking strategies to the other phases like the projection and gating operations to roughly double their unit counts. Now I'm looking at the MLA layers and noticing the router is bottlenecked—only block 0 is doing real work while the others idle, and the serial thread-0 scan over the top-8 logits is taking 16-20µs, which seems slow for what should be a simple reduction across 288 elements per thread. The issue is likely the weight load: one CTA pulling 295KB of router weights at ~50GB/s, which takes about 6µs, then softmax and barrier synchronization while 187 CTAs sit idle. I could parallelize by spreading the logits computation across 8 CTAs (one per expert group) then having a single CTA handle the top-8 selection, but that adds another barrier. The real constraint is that router logits depend on hattn from the previous oproj phase, so they can't start until that's done—but maybe I can overlap the router computation with oproj itself by computing logits from the projection output as it's being written. Actually, a cleaner approach: distribute the logits computation across 32 CTAs (2 experts per CTA, full 2304 dot product per warp) and write results to global memory, then have each CTA redundantly compute the top-8 selection from those global logits at the start of the next phase. Each CTA would do ~512 dependent comparisons, taking maybe 2-5µs, but since they all run in parallel it only adds ~2µs overhead to the gateup phase instead of requiring a separate synchronization barrier. Organizing the router phase into 16 units with 4 experts each, where each unit handles 512 threads strided across experts, writing the 64 logits to global memory, and computing rmsnorm per-unit over the 2304 dimensions — roughly 21 units total for about 3-4 microseconds. Then moving into the gateup phase where thread 0 computes the top-8 selections and weights into shared memory for all units to access. The top-8 IDs are computed redundantly across CTAs since they're deterministic from the same logits, giving bitwise-identical results — this recomputation in the down phase costs about 1-2 microseconds of serial overhead per CTA but nets a 15-microsecond win per layer, scaling to 60 microseconds across 4 layers. The baseline Z latency of 21 microseconds is mostly launch overhead; the pybind wrapper is already minimal, passing just the hidden tensor and 3 integers, so the cooperative kernel launch itself might account for 10-15 microseconds — need to profile an empty coop kernel loop to isolate that. Looking at the phase timing breakdown, the 21.5-microsecond cumulative cost includes the Python loop, pybind marshaling, kernel launch, Z phase execution, one barrier, and exit. If launch overhead is around 18 microseconds, that's a fixed tax per iteration. Cooperative launches are historically expensive, so I'm considering whether to ditch the cooperative kernel entirely and implement a software global barrier using atomic counters instead — still a single kernel, but with manual grid synchronization that works as long as one CTA per SM is guaranteed co-resident. Plain launches of 188 CTAs at 1 CTA/SM should be co-resident if the grid size doesn't exceed the SM count and preemption doesn't occur, which is standard practice in persistent kernels though it carries deadlock risk if occupancy assumptions break. The occupancy check should protect against that. I'm not even sure if cooperative launch is actually slower than normal launch, so I need to measure that first. My experiment plan is to measure: the gemv g-loop with unroll factor 2, splitting work units to fill all 188 CTAs across the A/C/E/F and waveform phases, then parallelizing the router phase with redundant top-8 computation in E/F, and finally investigating launch overhead. I'll combine A, B, and C in one code pass, then benchmark. For the router phase rewrite, I need to ensure logits are in bf16 with proper rounding before writing, weights are computed from global logits, and the probability normalization uses a full softmax sum across all 64 logits—each CTA computes from the complete set. I'm adding a new scratch buffer for the logits and updating the C++ struct and Python bindings accordingly, then replacing the moe_router phase with units that compute logits for 4 experts per unit with 128 threads per expert, striding over the 2304 dimension with recomputed x for each unit. For the implementation, I'm recomputing rmsnorm per unit by reading the attention output, rounding to bf16, and using block-wide sum reduction before each thread computes its slice into shared memory and performs the dot product. Unit 16 writes the normalized x to global memory and can be merged as one of the 16 units, while units 16–19 handle x preloading alongside the existing 4 copy units, bringing the total to 20 units. Before the gemv consumers in moe_gateup and moe_down, thread 0 computes the top-8 indices and weights into shared memory. For the shared memory layout, I'm carving out a region for the gate sum prep with ids and weights arrays, placing xs at offset 0 (8KB), gate sum at 8192 (128B), and the reduction/ids arrays starting at 8320, which fits within the available space. In gateup, I prep xs with a syncthreads, then thread 0 serially computes the top-8 using exponentials and scans before the unit loop, while moe_down's per-unit loop re-preps xs each iteration but computes top-8 only once beforehand. Now I'm realizing the router is doing redundant top-8 computations across phases and layers — roughly 8 redundant calculations of 1-3 microseconds each, where the serial softmax over 64 logits plus 8 passes of 64 comparisons adds up to around 5 microseconds per computation. I should clean up the scratch entries by removing the ids/wts fields entirely and adding a logits array instead, which simplifies the struct edits. The serial approach is too slow, so I'm switching to a warp-parallel top-8 selection where each lane handles two experts and we iterate 8 times with warp-max-reduce operations using shuffle instructions — this brings the cost down to roughly 0.2 microseconds, which is much faster. After selecting the top-8 ids and values, I need to compute the softmax weights using the formula wts_j = exp(l_j − mx), which requires finding the maximum logit first. For the softmax normalization, I'm computing the exponentials for each lane's two candidates, reducing the sum across the warp with shuffle operations, then normalizing by dividing by the sum plus a small epsilon to avoid division by zero. The final weight formula accounts for the fact that we're selecting from the full probability distribution and rescaling to match the reference implementation's behavior — this ensures the result is deterministic and identical across all CTAs. Now moving into the device function that warp 0 executes while other threads wait at the synchronization barrier. The logits phase processes 16 units across 4 experts, with each unit recomputing RMSNorm over the shared memory buffer. I'm organizing 512 threads as 4 expert groups of 128 threads each, where each thread accumulates over its assigned dimension slice in 18 iterations, then reducing within each expert group using shared memory. After the reduction, I'm writing the accumulated logits in bfloat16 format, and for unit 0 specifically, also writing the xn_moe values in half precision. The layer copies continue using the same 4-unit preload pattern, and the gateup phase correctly reads from the xn_moe written by the logits unit. One issue to watch: the test script reads from scratch buffer index 6, which will need updating. Now I'm mapping out the unit splits for the different phases — kda_phaseA handles q/k/v/g with 256 gemv units plus a beta unit and 4 copy units, oproj_phase uses 144 units split by tile and kchunk, and gateup divides its 288 units by slot. For the remaining phases: down has 324 units with per-unit xs prep that's negligible despite the doubled cost, and mla_A starts with 96 units for q and 10 units for kv_a. The wv phase uses 64 units with two groups per kchunk, duplicating xs prep from context. The scores phase at 16k runs in 62µs across 257 units over 188 CTAs, creating imbalance — splitting into 32-row chunks would give 514 units requiring 3 rounds worst-case instead of the current 2-round approach. Comparing efficiency: the current 2-round setup achieves 68% utilization with 376 unit-slots for 257 units, while 32-row chunks would reach 91% with 564 slots for 514 units, potentially dropping to ~46µs. The key win is restructuring the scores unit to use 32-row chunks directly with a single batch loop, which halves the shared memory batches. This also means updating the max/sumexp context reading to work with the new 514-unit NCH2 chunks and adjusting the mla_ctx units to match the 32-row structure with p_s[32][32] staging. Now I'm looking at the bandwidth constraints: the context phase at 16k reads 16.8MB in ~25µs (0.67TB/s), and since both phases read the full cache, that's 50GB per step combined. Fusing scores and context to read the cache once isn't feasible because softmax requires the global max and sum across all chunks before weighting. Instead, I can use a flash-style online accumulation approach where each chunk computes its local softmax and partial context in one pass, storing per-chunk max/sum/context values, then merge them in a final rescale phase using the global statistics. The storage overhead is significant though — storing per-chunk context for all chunks at 32 heads × 512 dimensions in float32 would be 33MB, which is too large. Dropping to 32-row chunks brings it down to 16MB, which trades the original 16.8MB cache re-read for 32MB of scratch traffic — roughly breakeven at float32, better at half precision. But actually, keeping the cache in L2 might work better since 16.8MB fits in the 128MB L2, though the scores phase seems to be evicting it before the context phase re-reads it. The real bottleneck is the atomic add and exponential overhead. I should just split into smaller units and measure the actual performance. At 2048 dimensions, the scores phase takes 29.7µs versus 62µs at 16k, suggesting the per-CTA staging overhead is substantial — loading 32KB of query and key data into shared memory per CTA adds up to 6.8MB just for staging across all 188 CTAs. But at 2048 only 33 CTAs actually have work, so 155 are idle yet still staging data. I need to gate the staging so only CTAs with actual work load the data. For the mla.B kernel at 12.3µs with 257 units, each unit reads 8192 bytes of key-value data plus two 16KB blocks of bf16 scales, totaling 40KB per unit across 256 units — that's 10MB but mostly stays L2-hot since scale rows get reused across units sharing the same group index. The timing is acceptable. I could hoist the scales and rearrange the qabs loop so each thread loads scale/zero pairs as consecutive bf16 values, but that's a minor optimization so I'll skip it for now. The kda.B kernel shows 6.3µs at layer 2 versus essentially no difference at layer 0, which points to L2 pressure from the 6.3MB state across three layers. On continuation, the Z kernel takes 21µs due to launch overhead, zeroing context, and copying hidden state — I'll revisit that with D later. The router phases are running 17-20µs each but should drop to ~4µs with improvements, saving roughly 60µs total. The gateup kernel varies between 9.7-32µs depending on L2 behavior, though splits help. Now I'm going to edit the code starting with the gemv_ kernel. Adding pragma unroll directives... Capping the number of l values at 32 per thread with the same 16 half2 accumulators, and the barrier count stays at 29. The top8 efficiency looks good with warp0 handling it in about 0.3µs each direction. For the logits phase, unit 0 needs to write xn_moe after computing xh, and there's a barrier ensuring the gateup prep reads it correctly afterward — units 16-19 can safely copy the attention output since it's read-only and stored in shared memory per CTA. The edge case is that unit 0 handles experts 0-3 and the xn_moe write together. Now I'm implementing the top8 function that finds the maximum logit across a warp using a reduction, computes softmax weights with exponentials, and aggregates the sum... Continuing with the iterative selection loop that finds the best expert 8 times, using a warp-wide max reduction to identify the winner, and handling ties by preferring the lower expert index... Writing kernel logic... Verifying the group splits across all operations: oproj handles 32 groups correctly with 8 chunks of 4, gateup distributes 18 groups as 9+9, down uses 8 groups as 2×4, and the remaining kernels align properly. Ready to apply all the edits now.

reasoning
I'm spotting a critical race condition in the split-K implementation: kchunk 0 does a non-atomic write to the output buffer while kchunk 1 uses atomic operations, but there's no ordering guarantee between them. The atomic add from kchunk 1 could execute before kchunk 0's plain store, causing the accumulated value to get overwritten and lost. The fix is to zero the destination buffers beforehand and make both chunks atomic. I can leverage idle CTAs during the moe_logits phase to zero hg/hu — there are plenty of idle units available and it's just 18K floats. The same zeroing strategy applies to the qkvg buffer in the kda_phaseA split, which I can zero during the preceding phase. This eliminates the race by ensuring both atomic operations start from a clean slate. I'm realizing the scheduling gets tricky with the barrier between phases—moe_down of the previous layer runs before kda_phaseA of the next layer, so zeroing qkvg in phase Z for layer 0 works cleanly. But splitting kda_phaseA's k-dimension creates an imbalance: the first round uses 261 units (over the 188 limit), forcing a second round with only 73 units, which leaves significant idle capacity. The real question is whether splitting actually gains anything—each unit becomes half the work, so round time halves and two rounds roughly equal the original latency, but occupancy improves marginally (261 unit-slots versus 266 for the unsplit case). The MLP per CTA doesn't increase since the per-warp loop shrinks from 18 to 9 iterations, keeping in-flight work the same per iteration. What changes is CTA count active, pushing utilization from 71% toward something better. Looking at concurrent load streams: unsplit gives 133 CTAs × 16 warps × 4 loads in flight, which is substantial but the unroll-2 optimization (8 loads per warp) helps more. Splitting only makes sense where unit count is much smaller than 188 or remainder imbalance is severe—so I'd split oproj (72→144), mla_wv (32→64), and mla_A (57→110), but gateup at 144→288 is borderline since 144 CTAs already fill most of an SM. For gateup specifically, the measured 32µs for 21MB is the bottleneck, and splitting could theoretically reach 24.6µs (77% occupancy), but that's only ~23% gain against the complexity of zeroing and extra atomics in round 2. I'll do the split anyway since zeroing in the logits phase is straightforward and saves ~5µs per layer across 4 layers. For down at 162→324, the split adds prep duplication and atomicAdd is already in the write path, so the benefit is less clear. Instead of splitting with atomic operations, I could use separate partial buffers and have consumers sum them — no zeroing, no races, no atomics needed. For gateup, I'd add hg2/hu2 buffers and the down phase just computes the activation with both values summed. For kda A, I'd add a small qkvg2 buffer that phase B reads alongside the main qkvg, then adds them together. The barrier between phases handles ordering naturally. Now looking at how phase B reads from these split buffers — graw combines qkvg and qkvg2, while conv val does the same per channel. Beta uses xs instead so that's already fine. For the output projection, I'm keeping the existing atomic approach into the preloaded residual, but for wv I need to split the work across chunks using the partial buffer trick — adding an obuf2 buffer so the mla operation can read from the prepped data without conflicts. The tricky part is that KDA phase B only writes to obuf (no obuf2), so I need to make sure obuf2 stays zeroed for the KDA path. Another option is splitting by columns instead of rows — since wv output spans 32 heads with 128 channels each, I could distribute the work differently across the groups. Actually, the simplest approach is to give KDA's oproj a variant that reads only obuf, while MLA's version reads both obuf and obuf2. I can modify the prep function to accept an optional second source parameter that KDA passes as null. The same pattern could extend to the gateup→down path where the down prep would read from both hg and hg2, hu and hu2. For KDA's A→B path, it reads qkvg and qkvg2. I need to add scratch space for qkvg2 (4*C), hg2, hu2 (9*1024 each), obuf2 (C), and logits (64), while removing ids and weights. This requires reordering the scratch struct and syncing the C++ definition with the Python unpacking code. Updating the KScratch struct... Updating the remaining consumers: moe_gateup selects between the appropriate gate and up buffers based on the chunk flag, moe_down prep combines the respective halves from both buffers, mla_wv handles the output buffer selection with per-head offsets while reusing the full staging for both chunks, and oproj_phase adds a second source parameter for the prep step. I'm modifying prep_bf16 to accept an optional second source buffer, summing values from both when present, then rounding and storing the result. For KDA's 9-group chunk split, the zero-point correction still applies correctly since gemv operates per-group within the assigned range. But MLA's q unit split creates a race condition — two chunks write to the same destination without atomics, so I need either a partial buffer for q6144 or atomic operations to handle this safely. Now I'm adding the partial buffers for the consumers: qn staging and qr both read from q6144, so they'll need to incorporate q6144b values. reasoning
I was worried about a critical bug with early returns in the device function, but actually it's fine — when idle CTAs return from `mla_scores`, they're only exiting that function, not the entire kernel, so they still hit the synchronization point at the end of the phase. The comment I added is a bit misleading but the logic is sound. Now I'm checking the grid synchronization semantics: `grid.sync()` doesn't require all threads to be at the exact same instruction, just that all blocks eventually reach *a* grid.sync call, which they do. The early return is uniform across the entire CTA based on the block index, so there's no `__syncthreads()` divergence within a block. ✓ Now I need to think through the `mla_ctx` setup.
