assistant
reasoning
The unroll optimization didn't actually help—performance is slightly worse without it. The real regression comes from the splits themselves affecting the gateup/down operations. When we halve the number of elements per unit, we're halving the useful work relative to fixed overhead, and split-K compounds this by duplicating the scale and zero row loads while adding extra partial buffer traffic. The critical issue is how split-K changes the weight loading pattern—when we split across K chunks, different CTAs end up reading the same 128-column tile. Looking at the math more carefully: the gateup phase time should theoretically stay the same (288 units across 188 CTAs gives the same total work as before), and the down phase should also be equivalent. But the actual bottleneck is the per-unit silu prep—it went from 162 preps to 324 preps, and each prep now reads 4×1024 floats from global memory instead of 2×1024, plus the expf and synchronization overhead. That's 4× the prep traffic compared to before. Meanwhile, gateup writes doubled because we're now writing both hg and hg2, and the atomicAdd operations in down also doubled. The kda A split has similar issues with doubled qkvg2 write and read traffic. Looking at the actual performance deltas: layer1 dropped from 24.6µs to 15.6µs (9µs worse), and gateup went from 29.7µs to 24.4µs (5.3µs worse). The overall benchmark regressed from 0.405ms to 0.436ms at 8192—that's a real 30µs loss. I'm trying to figure out what's actually causing this. The extra memory traffic from the writes and reads doesn't quite add up to 30-45µs at DRAM speeds. But the gemv call count roughly doubled per step, and each call carries overhead from syncthreads, the final reduce across 512 threads, and rescanning xs per group. The warp 0 overhead for the top8 reads is minimal—maybe 4µs across 8 phases while other warps continue in parallel. I'm thinking the cleanest path forward is to revert the gateup/down/kdaA splits to isolate what's actually causing the slowdown, especially since the DOWN split alone added ~38µs total. Let me check if that's the culprit before making further changes. Looking at the metrics now: gateup went from 24.4/32.1/19.4 to 19.2/29.7/42.4/30.4—that's a +36µs hit. The routers actually improved dramatically with the parallelization (73.2 down to 25.4), and kdaA stayed roughly flat, but the scores and context reads dropped nicely by 13µs. So the router gains are real, but gateup and down are both regressing significantly. The issue is specifically the gateup and down splits—I should revert those two back to unsplit while keeping the smem top8 optimization, the single buffers for hg/hu, and the down prep reads restricted to hg/hu only. The theory on why halving the unit size hurts gateup so much comes down to L2 and DRAM efficiency. With split-K, each CTA reads half the K dimension, but since the weight matrix is row-major, both CTAs end up streaming the same rows just at different times. The problem is they're likely executing out of sync within the phase, which breaks the sequential access pattern and causes cache misses. Actually, the real culprit might be DRAM page locality. A full-K unit lets one CTA walk through a contiguous region of the weight matrix, but it only touches a small slice of each row due to the column tiling. The eight tile-CTAs together cover the full matrix, and whether they stay synchronized in their row progress determines how efficiently they use the DRAM row buffer. With 144 CTAs running, that synchronization breaks down with the split approach.
assistant
reasoning
I've identified that the regression comes from the gateup and down splits causing DRAM locality issues when tile CTAs land in different rounds, not from unroll. I'll revert those two splits while keeping the other optimizations.
assistant

Looking at the scores/context specifically, I think I found the culprit. At 8192, switching to 32-row chunks increased the unit count from 129 (which fit in one round with 64-row chunks) to 257 units, forcing two rounds instead of one—that's a direct performance hit. The 64-row chunks were actually optimal for 8192, while 32-row chunks only helped at 2048. I need to make the chunk size adaptive: pick between 32 and 64 rows based on what minimizes the number of rounds for each problem size. But wait, at 16384 the math doesn't add up. With 32-row chunks I'd expect fewer row-times overall (96 vs 128), which should be faster, yet the benchmark shows it's slightly slower. The issue might be fixed overhead per unit—staging operations, synchronization, and the softmax pass happen once per CTA regardless of chunk size. Plus the context matrix operations scale with the number of chunks: 514 chunks instead of 257 means double the global memory traffic for the mx/sm computations. The extra bandwidth cost is around 7 microseconds at L2 speeds, which is negligible. I think the simplest fix is to revert chunk size back to 64 rows but keep the optimization that skips staging for idle CTAs—that's what gave the 2048 win. I could parameterize the code to support both 32 and 64-row chunks, though the 32-row version uses a single-batch staging approach that fits in shared memory, while 64-row needs the loop structure back. Maybe I should just restore the original two-batch design with the blockIdx guard and set chunk to 64, or even better, make it a runtime parameter so I can test both without recompiling. The key constraint is that the scores shared memory staging only accommodates 32 rows, so if I go with 64 rows I need to bring back the batching loop. I'll restore the older version with three tweaks: adaptively choose between 32 and 64 rows based on whether the grid can handle it, apply the idle-CTA skip, and make sure the mx/sm stride matches the chosen chunk size. Both the context phase and the main phase need to use the same chunk size calculation so they stay in sync—since they see the same grid dimensions, they'll compute identically. At 16384, the math suggests 32 rows should win (cost 96 vs 128), but measurements showed it performed worse, which might point to something else in the mx/sm pass within the context phase. I'm going to revert both the scores and context back to the exact old 64-row version, add just the idle-CTA staging skip, and remeasure. If 8192 bounces back to around 0.405, I'll know I'm on the right track, then test reverting the kda A split as well since its profile looked neutral anyway. Primary Request and Intent: Write `solution.py` in the current working directory (`/home/shadeform/kernelbench.com/benchmarks/mega/outputs/runs/20260719_121747_or-fable_anthropic_claude-fable-5_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode`) making batch-1 autoregressive decode of a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid block as fast as possible on RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7 ~1.8 TB/s). Explicit requirements: - Same `Model(cfg)` / `step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state)` interface as reference.py with identical parameter/buffer names so `sol_model.load_state_dict(ref_model.state_dict(), strict=True)` works. - Unit: 4 layers, pattern ("K","K","K","M") — 3 KDA + 1 MLA — each followed by 64-expert MoE (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), hidden 2304, RMSNorm (EPS 1e-6, fp32) + residuals. - W4A16 int4 group-128 asymmetric quant: `w_q` uint8 `(in//2, out)` (even-K low nibble, odd-K high), `scales`/`zeros` bf16 `(in//128, out)`; dequant fused into every GEMV; never materialize the bf16 weight. - HARD MEGAKERNEL REQUIREMENT: the entire timed decode step must be a SINGLE GPU kernel launch invoked exactly once per `step()`. CUDA graphs, torch.compile, per-op kernel loops banned (advisory tripwires + post-run authenticity judge reads the final solution.py and every local module it imports; obfuscation itself is a red flag). check.py hard-fails AST-matched imports of: transformers, flash_mla, flashmla, fla.ops, flash_linear_attention, vllm, sglang, bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, auto_gptq, autoawq, awq, exllama, reference, baseline — scanned recursively over solution.py + local modules it imports including `scratch/*.py` sidecars. - Correctness: cosine ≥ 0.98 vs reference for `out`, `st[0]["S"]`, `st[3]["c_kv"]` over seeds (0,1,2) × ctx (2048, 8192), identical fed state, one step. "If `python check.py` has not printed PASS, you are not done." - Scoring: geomean of baseline_ms/solution_ms over ctx 2048 (32 steps), 8192 (16), 16384 (16); benchmark.py gates on cosine ≥ 0.98 (seed 7, `out` only) then best-of-3 trials. "Take as long as you need to actually push the latency down." - Workspace isolation (verbatim): "you are already running inside the archive-local problem workspace ... Do not write to /home/shadeform/kernelbench.com/benchmarks/mega/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode or to the source repository's problems/ tree." 2. Key Technical Concepts: - Single cooperative CUDA kernel: `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel`, 188 CTAs × 512 threads (SM count × occupancy at runtime), `cg::this_grid().sync()` between 29 phases, 80KB (81920B) dynamic smem, `__launch_bounds__(512, 1)`, single by-value `KArg` struct param. - Phase sequence (ph counter, `PHASE_END()` = grid.sync + early-exit on `debug_max_phase`): Z(1) → per KDA layer i∈{0,1,2}: A=qkvg GEMVs+beta+hattn preload(133 units), B=conv/silu/decay/state-update(128 units), oproj(144 units, atomicAdd into residual-preloaded hattn), moe_logits(20 units), gateup(144 units), down(162 units) → MLA: A(110 units), B(257 units), scores(64-row chunks), ctx(64-row chunks), wv(64 units), oproj(144) → moe3 → tail. max_phase ≥ 29 for full run. - int4 GEMV unit: 16 warps × 4 packed rows per 128-k group, lane ↔ 4 cols (uint32 load), fp16 magic dequant `deq2(w,sh) = ((w>>sh)&0x000F000F)|0x64006400` then `__hsub2` 1024; half2 accumulation vs smem-staged x prescaled by 1/8; per-group fp32 fold with scales/zeros; zero-point correction `−s·z·gsum[g]` by warp 0 ONLY; cross-warp reduce via 16×128 f32 smem; final `acc * 8 * wmul`. - NEW this session — parallel MoE router: `moe_logits` phase spreads the 64 router logit dot-products over 16 CTAs (4 experts each, 128 threads per expert, per-CTA rmsnorm recompute, bf16-rounded logits to global `A.s.logits`); consumers (gateup/down) each run `moe_top8` on warp 0 — deterministic identical results across CTAs from identical global logits; ties pick lowest expert index (matches torch.topk). Saved ~48µs/step total. - Split-K via partial buffers (NOT atomics) to avoid write-order races: producer chunks write disjoint buffers (q6144/q6144b, kva/kvab, obuf/obuf2), consumer sums then bf16-rounds — preserves reference precision since fp32 partials sum before rounding. - LESSON LEARNED: split-K unit splits on the big MoE GEMV phases (gateup 288, down 324, kda A 261) REGRESSED performance ~35µs each despite better occupancy — hypothesized DRAM row-buffer locality loss when a tile's k-chunks land in different scheduling rounds. Reverted. - LESSON: 32-row scores/ctx chunks regressed ctx 8192 (257 units = 2 uneven rounds vs 129 = 1 round with 64-row chunks). Reverted to 64-row; kept idle-CTA `return` skip (device-function return still reaches the caller's grid.sync — safe). - LESSON: `#pragma unroll 2` on gemv group loop did not help (marginally worse). - Benchmark run-to-run variance is ±3% (GPU verified idle via nvidia-smi); single runs unreliable for A/B decisions. Phase-profile deltas contaminated by L2 hotness of truncated runs (128MB L2 vs 214MB weight working set). - Profile (post-batch, ctx 2048): routers 4.2/4.2/4.8/12.2µs; gateup 19-42µs; down 17-26µs; scores 18µs; ctx 11µs; Z+launch overhead ~20.5µs; total ~389µs. - Constants: KDA_SCALE=0.08838834764831845, MLA_SCALE=0.07216878364870323, D_HID=2304, C_KDA=4096, NH=32, DK=128, M_INTER=1024, HQ_MLA=6144, KVL=512, QR=64, KVA=576, NKVB=8192, NBLK=512, SMEM_BYTES=81920. - No fast-math (RoPE at pos ~16.9k); torch's `-D__CUDA_NO_HALF_OPERATORS__` → intrinsics only; decay = sigmoid(−g); bf16 rounding points match reference. 3. Files and Code Sections: - `solution.py` — the deliverable, heavily edited this session. Current live state: - `KScratch` struct (26 pointers, order = C++ unpack = python `_alloc`): x, hattn, qkvg, qkvg2, betaout, obuf, obuf2, xn_moe, logits, hg, hu, hg2, hu2, q6144, q6144b, kva, kvab, qabs, qr, scores, mx, sm, ctx, outb, ckv, krope. `self._out = self._scr[23].view(2304)`. `nch = cap // 32 + 2`. NOTE: qkvg2, hg2, hu2 are now DEAD (allocated but unused after reverts) — candidates for cleanup before final. - `moe_logits(A, layer, SM)` (replaces moe_router): u<16 → per-CTA rmsnorm into smem xh + 4 expert dots (el = threadIdx.x>>7, t7 = threadIdx.x&127, stride-128 dot, warp shfl + er[el*4+(t7>>5)] smem reduce, t7==0 writes bf16-rounded `A.s.logits[u*4+el]`); u==0 also writes xn_moe; u 16..19 copy x[l+1] ← hattn. - `moe_top8(const float* lg, float routed, int* ids_s, float* wts_s)`: warp-0 only; lane holds logits lane/lane+32; warp-max for mx, warp-sum for softmax denom; 8 iterations of warp argmax with min-index tiebreak; `__syncwarp()` then lane<8 scales wts_s by `routed/(wsum+1e-9)`. - `moe_gateup`: 144 units (slot = u>>4, gu = (u>>3)&1, tile = u&7, g0=0 ng=18), ids_s = (int*)(SM+8320), wts_s = (float*)(SM+8352), `if (threadIdx.x < 32) moe_top8(...)` before xs fill; writes hg/hu non-atomic. - `moe_down`: 162 units (slot = u/18, tile = u%18, g0=0 ng=8), same smem top8; silu prep reads hg/hu only; atomicAdd into x[layer+1]; wmul = wts_s[slot] for routed, 1.f shared. - `kda_phaseA`: 133 units (reverted): u<128 → mat = u>>5, tile = u&31, full-K gemv into qkvg; u==128 beta; u 129-132 hattn preload copies (`j0 = (u - 129) * 576`). - `kda_phaseB`: reads qkvg only (qkvg2 sums reverted out). - `oproj_phase(A, wq, sc, zr, dst, src2, SM)`: 144 units, `tile = u % 18, kchunk = u / 18`, `gemv_i4(..., kchunk * 4, 4, ...)` atomicAdd into dst; `prep_bf16(A.s.obuf, src2, C_KDA, xs, gsum)`. Called with src2=nullptr (KDA) / A.s.obuf2 (MLA). - `prep_bf16(const float* src, const float* src2, int n, __half* xs, float* gsum)`: `float v = src[i] + (src2 ? src2[i] : 0.f);` then bf16 round, ×0.125. - `mla_phaseA`: 110 units: u<96 → tile=u>>1, kchunk=u&1, gemv(HQ_MLA, kchunk*9, 9) into q6144/q6144b; u<106 → v=u-96, tile=v>>1, kchunk=v&1, nc=min(128, KVA-tile*128), into kva/kvab; else copies `j0 = (u - 106) * 576`. - `mla_phaseB`: qn staging reads `A.s.q6144[hh*192+dd] + A.s.q6144b[hh*192+dd]`; ckv append reads `A.s.kva[i] + A.s.kvab[i]`; krope reads kva+kvab sums; qr reads `A.s.q6144[b0] + A.s.q6144b[b0]` where `b0 = hh * 192 + 128 + 2 * r`. - `mla_scores`: 64-row chunks (`NCH = (L1 + 63) >> 6`), two-batch (b=0,1) 32-row smem staging, `if (blockIdx.x >= NCH) return;` idle skip before qabs/qr staging; smem: qa_s@0, qr_s@32768, ckv_s@36864, red 64*32 @69632, kr_s@77824. - `mla_ctx`: 64-row chunks, `if (blockIdx.x >= NCH) return;`, p_s 64*32 @SM+4096, Msm/Sinv computed per participating CTA. - `mla_wv`: 64 units: hh = u>>1, kchunk = u&1, `gemv_i4(..., kchunk * 2, 2, hh * 256 + 128, 128, ...)` into `(kchunk ? A.s.obuf2 : A.s.obuf) + hh * 128`. - `gemv_i4`: no unroll pragma (reverted). - Kernel body calls: `moe_logits(A, layer, SM)` / `moe_logits(A, 3, SM)`; oproj calls carry the src2 arg. - Host unpack includes qkvg2/obuf2/logits/hg2/hu2/q6144b/kvab entries in order. - `scratch/time_phases.py` — per-phase timing via debug_max_phase; NAMES list; `time_k(model, cfg, ctx, k, steps=64)` with 8 warmup steps. - `scratch/test_seeds.py` — seeds 0-10 × ctx {2048,8192,16384} out-cosine sweep; prints worst. - `scratch/test_experts.py` — BROKEN now (reads `sol_model._scr[6][:8]` for ids, which no longer exist as scratch); served its purpose, left broken deliberately. - `scratch/test_stages.py` — ctx 64 fresh + chained step 2 comparison; all cosines ≥ 0.9999 after the batch. - Contract files: reference.py, check.py, benchmark.py, shapes.py, problem.yaml (read in prior session). 4. The profile numbers claimed the router phases dropped and the change saved ~48µs total, but that's probably an artifact of L2 cache contamination in the cumulative timing methodology. assistant
reasoning
Looking at the original moe_router structure, it stores ids and weights in A.s.ids and A.s.wts, but the current KScratch has logits instead. Rather than fully reverting the struct, I can keep the cheap parallel logits phase that's already profiled well, then consolidate the top-8 selection into a single point—maybe a CTA at u==16 or by extending moe_logits to handle the selection after computing log probabilities. The challenge is that 16 different CTAs write logits, so top-8 selection needs all of them, which requires a grid sync. That's why consumers currently do it redundantly. A hybrid approach would keep parallel logit computation in moe_logits but move top-8 to a single location, though the latency cost of waiting for that selection before GEMV work starts might offset the savings. The simplest path is probably to restore the original moe_router—one CTA doing everything into global ids and weights—and have consumers read from global memory, which matches the known-20.32 configuration. I need to add scratch space for int32 ids. The profiling showed parallel logits at 4.2µs versus the original router at 12-16µs, and the summary claims a ~48µs batch savings, but if reverting the router restores 20.32, that win might be illusory and the redundant top-8 in consumers is actually costing real time. So the concrete plan is to replace the logits pointer with ids and weights pointers in KScratch, restore the original moe_router that writes to those global locations, and then update moe_gateup and moe_down to read ids and weights from global instead of computing top-8 in shared memory. Now I need to look at the gateup and down kernels, plus the host-side scratch allocation and unpacking logic, to see what changes are needed there.

I'm considering whether to test the parallel logits phase without the redundant consumer top-k operation. The core tension is that parallelizing logit computation across 16 CTAs saves time on the router phases (down from 12-16µs to ~4µs per layer), but it creates a dependency problem—consumers need the top-k IDs at phase start. Adding a grid sync to fold the top-8 computation into the end of the logits phase would cost 1.5-2µs in barrier overhead versus the 8-12µs router savings, so it might still be worth it. Another angle is to split the top-8 work into the gateup phase itself, having CTA0 compute IDs while other CTAs start their GEMV work on the shared expert slots that don't depend on routing decisions, but the routed slots still need IDs immediately. I'm also considering whether the router computation itself can start earlier by overlapping with a prior phase—the router input (hattn) comes from the oproj phase via atomicAdd, so it can't begin until oproj finishes. The oproj phase has 44 idle CTAs out of 188, but they can't help because they'd still be waiting for hattn to be ready. A simpler idea: keep the original single-CTA router design but optimize it—one CTA reads 2304 values for RMSnorm plus 64×2304 BF16 dot products (about 295KB of router weights), which at ~50GB/s per-CTA should take ~6µs, plus a trivial serial top-8 scan. The profiled time of 12-16µs suggests the other 183 CTAs sitting idle for that duration is the real bottleneck. So maybe the answer is to keep the parallel logits approach but use a faster reduction for the top-8 step. The potential speedup could push throughput from 20.47 to around 21.2 tokens per second, which seems worth exploring. The tradeoff is adding four more phases (one per layer), bumping the phase count from 29 to 33, but the consumer kernels stay cleaner without the top8 logic mixed in. The real constraint is that top8 can't fold into the logits phase without a grid sync since 16 different CTAs are writing logits with no cross-CTA visibility otherwise—unless I collapse back to a single CTA computing all logits, which defeats the parallelization. So the plan is to split into two phases: one with 16 CTAs handling the logits computation plus 4 copy CTAs, then a separate top8 phase. The grid synchronization between phases adds about 1.5-2µs overhead, and the top8 kernel itself has most CTAs idle for another 1-2µs, but the overall savings from parallelizing the router should net out to around 5-8µs per token, which compounds to 20-32µs total savings across the batch. Now I need to add back the scratch logits buffer to the struct and restructure the kernel phases: moe_logits computes the logits into global memory, then a new moe_pick phase runs the warp-parallel top8 selection on thread0 of CTA0, writing the selected indices and weights back to global. The kernel body chains these phases with synchronization barriers before moving into gateup. Python sets the debug default to 1<<30 for max_phase, giving us 33 total phases (29 existing plus 4 new ones), and the host launcher passes this through model.debug_max_phase. The tie-breaking in the warp-parallel top8 matches torch.topk by minimum index, though there's a slight numerical difference in the cosine similarity (0.9879 serial vs 0.9966 warp) due to bf16 rounding during logits accumulation—both implementations pass validation.

Key Technical Concepts: - Single cooperative CUDA kernel: `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel`, 188 CTAs × 512 threads, `cg::this_grid().sync()` between phases (now 33 phases), 80KB dynamic smem, `__launch_bounds__(512, 1)`, single by-value `KArg` struct. - Phase sequence (33 phases, `PHASE_END()` = grid.sync + early exit on `debug_max_phase`): Z → per KDA layer i∈{0,1,2}: A (133 units qkvg GEMVs+beta+hattn preload), B (conv/silu/decay/state update), oproj (144 units, tile=u%18, kchunk=u/18, ng=4, atomicAdd), logits, pick, gateup (144 units), down (162 units) → MLA: A (57 units, non-split: u<48 q6144 full-K ng=18; u<53 kva; else copies), B (257 units, no partial sums), scores (64-row chunks + idle warm), ctx (64-row chunks + idle skip), wv (32 units, ng=4, full-K into obuf), oproj → moe3 logits/pick/gateup/down → tail. - **THE KEY DISCOVERY THIS SESSION — L2 warming during bandwidth-idle phases**: `prefetch.global.L2` PTX from idle CTAs during phases with little DRAM traffic (moe_logits, moe_pick, mla_scores idle chunks) is a massive win (19.57→22.90). Prefetching during bandwidth-HEAVY phases (inside gemv_i4, gateup idle slots, down idle slots) consistently HURTS — it competes with the phase's own demand reads. - Two-phase router: moe_logits (16 CTAs × 4 experts each, per-CTA rmsnorm, 128-thread dots, bf16-rounded to global `A.s.logits`; CTAs 16-19 copy residual; CTAs ≥20 L2-warm) + moe_pick (warp 0 of CTA 0: warp-parallel softmax + 8× argmax with min-index tiebreak into global `A.s.ids`/`A.s.wts`; all other CTAs L2-warm shared gate/up). The prior design (per-consumer redundant top-8 in gateup/down) was a ~0.8x regression; the single-phase spin-counter fold was also worse (loses the pick phase's free warm window). - int4 GEMV unit (`gemv_i4`): 16 warps × 4 packed rows per 128-k group, lane ↔ 4 cols, fp16 magic dequant, half2 accumulation vs smem-staged x prescaled 1/8, warp-0-only zero-point correction, 16×128 f32 smem reduce. - Benchmark variance ±1-3% run-to-run on idle GPU; A/B decisions need the signal to exceed that. - Phase profile at ctx 2048 (current 22.90 config, total 334µs): Z 20.6µs (launch), kda2.A 23.6, moe2.gateup 28.0, mla.scores 31.3, moe1.gateup 21.1, moe3.gateup 19.1, moe1.down 14.7, moe2.down 14.2, mla.A 14.9, mla.B 13.2, kda0.A ≈0 (L2-hot), kda0/1 phases nearly free. - Constants: KDA_SCALE=0.08838834764831845, MLA_SCALE=0.07216878364870323, D_HID=2304, C_KDA=4096, NH=32, DK=128, M_INTER=1024, HQ_MLA=6144, KVL=512, QR=64, KVA=576, NKVB=8192, NBLK=512, SMEM_BYTES=81920. - No fast-math; torch's `-D__CUDA_NO_HALF_OPERATORS__` → intrinsics only; bf16 rounding points match reference. 3. Files and Code Sections: - `solution.py` — the deliverable. Current live state (peak_fraction 22.90, check PASS): - `KScratch` (22 entries, struct order = C++ unpack order = python `_alloc` order): x(0), hattn(1), qkvg(2), betaout(3), obuf(4), xn_moe(5), ids(6, int32[8]), wts(7, f32[8]), logits(8, f32[64]), hg(9), hu(10), q6144(11), kva(12), qabs(13), qr(14), scores(15), mx(16), sm(17), ctx(18), outb(19), ckv(20), krope(21). `self._out = self._scr[19].view(2304)`. All dead partial buffers (qkvg2, obuf2, hg2, hu2, q6144b, kvab) removed. - `l2_warm` helper (defined just before "// Router phase A" comment): ```c __device__ void l2_warm(const uint8_t* const* bases, const size_t* sizes, int nreg, int pu, int np) { size_t total = 0; for (int i = 0; i < nreg; ++i) total += sizes[i]; size_t b0 = total * pu / np, b1 = total * (pu + 1) / np; for (size_t off = b0 + (size_t)threadIdx.x * 128; off < b1; off += (size_t)blockDim.x * 128) { size_t o = off; for (int i = 0; i < nreg; ++i) { if (o < sizes[i]) { asm volatile("prefetch.global.L2 [%0];" :: "l"(bases[i] + o)); break; } o -= sizes[i]; } } } ``` - `moe_logits(A, layer, SM)`: u<16 → per-CTA rmsnorm into smem xh (u==0 also writes xn_moe), 4 expert dots each (el=threadIdx.x>>7, t7=threadIdx.x&127, stride-128, shfl + er smem reduce), t7==0 writes bf16-rounded `A.s.logits[u*4+el]`; u 16..19 copy x[l+1] ← hattn (j0=(u-16)*576); u≥20 → l2_warm with regions: moe_wq[layer][5] (512×2304 shared down) + layer<2: kda_wq[layer+1][0..1] full (SZ=1152*C_KDA each); layer==2: mla_wq[0] (1152*HQ_MLA); layer==3: kda_wq[0][0..1] full. Called `l2_warm(bases, sizes, nreg, u - 20, gridDim.x - 20)`. - `moe_pick(A, layer)`: blockIdx.x != 0 → warm shared gate/up (BLK=1152*1024, 2*BLK split over gridDim.x-1 CTAs, inline prefetch loop); CTA 0 warp 0 → warp-parallel softmax + top-8 (v0=lg[lane], v1=lg[lane+32]; warp max/sum; 8 iterations argmax with min-index tiebreak; lane==0 writes A.s.ids[j]/A.s.wts[j]; final lane<8 scale by routed/(wsum+1e-9)). - `moe_gateup`: 144 units (slot=u>>4, gu=(u>>3)&1, tile=u&7), reads `A.s.ids[slot]` global, full-K `gemv_i4(wq, sc, zr, M_INTER, 0, 18, tile*128, 128, ...)` non-atomic into hg/hu. - `moe_down`: 162 units (slot=u/18, tile=u%18), silu prep from hg/hu, `gemv_i4(..., D_HID, 0, 8, ...)` atomicAdd into x[layer+1]; wmul = `A.s.wts[slot]` routed / 1.f shared. - `oproj_phase(A, wq, sc, zr, dst, src2, SM)`: 144 units, tile=u%18, kchunk=u/18, `gemv_i4(..., kchunk*4, 4, ...)` atomicAdd; `prep_bf16(A.s.obuf, src2, C_KDA, xs, gsum)`; both call sites pass src2=nullptr now. - `mla_phaseA`: 57 units: u<48 full-K gemv (0,18) into q6144; u<53 kva tiles (nc=min(128, KVA-tile*128)); else hattn copies j0=(u-53)*576. - `mla_phaseB`: 257 units; qn/ckv/krope/qr read q6144/kva directly (no partial sums). - `mla_scores`: NCH=(L1+63)>>6 64-row chunks; **idle warm**: `if (blockIdx.x >= NCH && (int)gridDim.x > NCH) { bases[1]={A.w.mla_wq[3]}; sizes[1]={(size_t)2048*2304}; l2_warm(..., blockIdx.x-NCH, gridDim.x-NCH); }` followed by the existing `if (blockIdx.x >= NCH) return;` idle skip. - `mla_ctx`: 64-row chunks, `if (blockIdx.x >= NCH) return;` (ctx-phase warming was tried and removed). - `mla_wv`: 32 units, u=head, full-K `gemv_i4(..., NKVB, 0, 4, u*256+128, 128, ..., A.s.obuf + u*128, ...)`. - `phase_Z`: hidden load + ctx zero + fresh-state cache copies (no done counter — that was reverted). - Kernel body per KDA layer: A, B, oproj, logits, pick, gateup, down (7 phases ×3); reasoning
I'm working through the kernel design for the scores computation, where 512 threads are organized as 32 rows by 16 half2-heads, with each thread handling a 512-dimensional dot product plus a 64-dimensional rope dot in half2 precision. Per 32-row batch, each thread performs around 576 half-precision fused multiply-adds, and with two batches per chunk at NCH=32 (context length 2048), each CTA processes one chunk in about 31.3 microseconds. The staging loop loads data efficiently across 512 threads with 16 iterations each, pulling bfloat16 data from the L2-hot global memory. The compute phase chains dependent half-precision operations with dual accumulators, but the sequential dependency latency alone shouldn't account for the full 31-microsecond timing — there's likely overhead from grid synchronization barriers and the fact that only 32 CTAs are actively computing while 156 others are warming the cache and waiting at the barrier. The phase time reflects the maximum across all CTAs, and the busy ones are reading the cached key-value data from global memory, which at 2048 context length spreads to about 64KB per CTA. I'm exploring an alternative approach: instead of concentrating work on 32 CTAs, distribute across all available CTAs with finer-grained chunks and let the softmax reduction handle the aggregation across more chunks. The key constraint is NCHMAX — the maximum number of chunks the reduction can handle. For a 2048 context, using 32-row chunks gives 64 chunks total, which is still underutilizing the 188 available CTAs. I could adapt the chunk size based on context length: smaller chunks for smaller contexts to stay within one round, larger chunks for bigger contexts to avoid regression from multiple rounds. The kernel currently hardcodes 2 batches of 32 rows each, but I need to generalize this with a CROWS variable that scales the batch size accordingly. For the shared memory allocation, NCHMAX needs to accommodate the worst case — with adaptive chunking, the maximum chunks at 16384 context is around 257, so that's the floor for the mx/sm array sizing. The real issue is that mla_ctx uses 64-row chunks hardcoded while scores might use adaptive chunking, and they both index into the same mx/sm arrays. I'm thinking about decoupling this: use NCH_S for the scores' adaptive chunking (which determines mx/sm indexing) and NCH_C for the context's own row-loop chunking (which stays at 64-row blocks). This way the context phase can reduce over all score chunks without forcing the same decomposition. For the memory layout, with CROWS=128 the red array would need 16KB but there's only 8KB allocated currently. Instead of enlarging red, I could accumulate max/sum per batch in registers for threads below 32, then compute the final per-chunk values at the end rather than storing all rows in shared memory. The approach is to keep red at 32×32 floats (4KB), write batch results at fixed positions, and after each batch's synchronization, have the first 32 threads merge the batch's max and sum into running accumulators using the standard online softmax merge formula with exponentials. For the context reduction, I need to replace the NCH parameter for the mx/sm reduction with NCH_S computed using the same adaptive CROWS logic. The expected performance improvement is substantial—scores should drop from 31.3 to around 12-16µs at context length 2048 with two CTAs, and the adaptive 128-row chunking strategy should also help at 16k where the current single-round limitation causes serialization. Now I'm implementing this carefully, starting with the current scores structure where NCH is computed as (L1+63)>>6, and I need to set up the shared memory layout for qa_s and qr_s. Writing kernel loop... Checking synchronization between the reduction threads and the next batch's compute phase — the staging sync at the loop start covers the barrier needed before the next batch writes to shared memory, and the reduction reads happen safely between the previous compute sync and the staging sync of the next iteration. Now I'm storing the final max and sum values for each chunk into the output arrays, then comparing this online merge approach against the original algorithm — the floating-point regrouping in the exponential is acceptable since softmax normalization in fp32 handles the tolerance well. For the row count selection, I'm computing how many rows to process per batch by checking if the chunk count fits within the grid dimensions, doubling the batch size until it does. Testing across different context lengths: at 2048 tokens with 32 rows per batch I get 65 chunks fitting in the available CTAs, leaving 123 warm blocks; at 8192 and 16384 I need to bump up to 64 and 128 rows respectively to stay within bounds. Looking at the memory layout, kda2.A reads about 9.4MB of weights, and the moe1.logits phase already prefetches those same buffers plus the shared down weights in 8.2 microseconds. The prefetch should drain through the L2 cache at around 1.8TB/s in about 6.5 microseconds, which seems plausible given that kda2.A runs roughly 36 microseconds after moe1's gateup and down phases complete. But here's the puzzle: those gateup and down phases stream 21MB through L2 in between, which could evict the warmed weights—though with a 128MB L2 on the Blackwell GPU, that shouldn't happen with only 21MB of intermediate data. Yet kda2.A is still slow at 23.6 microseconds when reading 9.4MB, which should only take about 5 microseconds if the data stayed L2-hot. I'm realizing the issue might be that in the truncated profile runs, each phase's data wasn't actually touched by previous iterations since we only execute up to phase k each time, so the warmth assumptions might not hold across the full execution. Actually, looking more carefully: when we run the same truncated step 64 times, each iteration does execute phases 1 through k, so phase k's reads should be hot across those iterations. The problem is that by the time we reach kda2.A (around phase 16), the cumulative working set of all previous phases—three KDA layers of qkvg weights (about 28MB), their output projections (about 14MB), and the MoE weights from two layers (around 42MB)—totals over 100MB, which exceeds the L2 capacity. So phase 16's data gets partially evicted, explaining why the late-phase reads look slow while early phases appear nearly free. The 23.6 microseconds for 9.4MB gives only 0.4TB/s throughput, which is poor for a GEMV operation across 133 units, when the theoretical bandwidth should deliver 5.2 microseconds at 1.8TB/s. The latency-bound behavior per CTA suggests each unit is reading its tile serially rather than in parallel. The MoE1 logits were supposed to warm the data, but the intervening gateup and gatedown streams (around 21MB each) are evicting it from cache—though 21MB shouldn't evict from a 128MB L2 unless the GPU has less L2 than expected. I'm trying to recall the exact L2 size for these cards: RTX PRO 6000 and RTX 5090 both use GB202, but the 5090 is a cut version with 96MB L2 instead of the full 128MB. Under truncation profiling, kda2.A shows 23.6 even though its own reads repeat every iteration, which means the 9.4MB tile gets evicted by earlier phases of the next iteration—phases 1 through 15 read roughly 90MB before kda2.A runs again, causing the eviction. So the cold-ish behavior per iteration makes sense. The 0.4TB/s throughput is puzzling though; with occupancy at 133 out of 188 CTAs, each unit's gemv is reading a 128-column tile across 1152 rows. The packed rows work out to 72KB per unit, so 133 units times 72KB gives the expected 9.4MB. Each CTA has 16 warps streaming 1152 rows across 4 groups, which should hide latency fine. But kda2.A follows the moe1.down phase, which ends with atomic adds and a barrier—the profile delta might be picking up barrier skew. Alternatively, the warm prefetches from moe1.logits could be the culprit: 12MB of prefetches over 5 to 8 microseconds drain into gateup, which is bandwidth-heavy and might be slowing it down. The timing difference between moe1 and moe0 gateup phases is hard to compare directly since moe0's faster time looks like an L2-hot artifact. For the moe gateup phases running at 21-28 microseconds, I'm analyzing the memory bandwidth: reading 8 routed experts plus shared weights totals around 21MB, which at ideal 1.8TB/s should take 11.8 microseconds, but moe2.gateup is hitting 28 microseconds at 0.75TB/s with 144 units distributed across 9 slots and 8 tiles. Looking at the actual kernel execution, each CTA processes one unit of 1152 rows by 128 columns, with 16 warps per CTA reading 72 rows each as 64-byte segments—exactly half a cache line, with adjacent tiles picking up the other half. The w_q layout stores elements row-major in (in/2, out) format, so each row reads 128 consecutive bytes as a full cache line, which checks out. The 0.75TB/s throughput is likely bottlenecked by dequantization compute or accumulation rather than memory access patterns. For the gateup kernel specifically, the 18 k-groups are distributed across 16 warps, so each warp handles one or two groups, with each group spanning 128 packed rows. The atomic addition traffic is the concern—each CTA writes out 32×512 floats per chunk, so increasing the number of chunks proportionally increases atomic operations. At the current 2048 context size with 32 chunks, that's 512K atomics, but scaling to 64 chunks would double that to 1M atomics, which could hurt performance. At the larger 16384 context size where we're currently doing 257 chunks in 2 rounds, the context phase is probably running about twice as slow as it should be. I could implement adaptive row chunking for the context phase too, using its own row loop with shared memory sized for up to 128 rows per chunk (8KB total), which still fits within the 80KB available per SM. The tradeoff is clear: at 16k the adaptive approach cuts atomics down significantly with 129 chunks, but at 2048 we're looking at more atomics with 65 chunks despite the parallelism gains. assistant
reasoning
I'm working through the memory access pattern for the kernel: every warp processes all ng groups, and within each group, warp w handles 4 packed rows at position rp = wq + (g*64 + w*4)*N. Since a group spans 64 packed rows and 16 warps × 4 rows equals that exactly, there's no load imbalance. Each lane ends up loading ng×4 uint32 values total—for ng=18, that's 72 loads per lane. The coalescing works out: 32 lanes × 4 bytes per load = 128 bytes per packed row, which is clean. With 4 unrolled loads per group iteration and 512 threads across 144 CTAs, there's substantial memory-level parallelism, yet throughput stalls at 0.75TB/s. The scales and zeros add another 12.5% traffic overhead since they're loaded separately per group from cold memory. I'm considering whether unrolling across multiple groups concurrently could help hide the ~600ns DRAM latency better than the current sequential group iteration, though the FMA dependency chain into the accumulators would still serialize the computation. Now I'm checking if warp scheduling can overlap the latency: with 16 warps per SM and one CTA per SM on the RTX PRO 6000's 188 SMs, the aggregate should reach ~0.98TB/s, but per-SM bandwidth is typically 13GB/s or higher, so something's limiting the effective throughput. Looking at per-thread memory-level parallelism with 4 outstanding loads and 600ns latency to see where the bottleneck actually sits. The key insight is that the group loop resets accumulators per iteration, meaning consecutive groups have independent dependency chains — unrolling by 2 would double the outstanding loads and let the SM better hide latency. I should add explicit unroll pragmas to encourage the compiler to interleave the loads, and then check the fill_gsum kernel where threads sequentially sum 128 half values from shared memory, which is a pure sequential bottleneck.

The core issue is that warming helps only when the intervening memory traffic is small, but each KDA layer pulls roughly 24MB per step (qkvg, output projection, and beta parameters), and that's before accounting for the MoE expert traffic. Calculating total per-step bandwidth: KDA layers contribute about 24MB each, MoE adds another 32MB per layer, and with multiple layers plus MLA, we're looking at roughly 240MB of traffic per step. The L2 cache is only 96-128MB, so warmed lines get evicted before they're reused. The distance from logits through gateup and down to kda.A is about 32MB of intervening traffic, which should mostly fit in L2, but prefetched lines might be inserted at lower priority and evicted first. There's a prefetch variant that marks lines as evict-last, which could help preserve the warmed data. I should try using the evict_last priority in the prefetch instruction to prevent streams from evicting these critical lines. The PTX ISA supports this syntax on sm_80 and newer, so it's worth testing. The tradeoff is that evict_last lines might linger and reduce effective L2 capacity for other streams, so I'll need to A/B test this approach.

Looking at the L2 miss pattern and latency — 32KB per CTA means 256 cache lines, and with 16KB in flight per CTA across 2 waves at 600ns each, that's about 1.2µs of latency. Currently each thread in the GEMV has 8 outstanding 4-byte loads from the unroll-2 × 4 rows, which gives 32B per thread or 1KB per warp and 16KB per CTA in flight. That last attempt made things worse, so I've reverted it. I've made solid progress this session—the score improved from 19.57 to 24.60—but I'm hitting diminishing returns. Before I finalize, I want to explore a couple more structural approaches: either splitting the kernel into multiple k-chunks across the tile grid, or reorganizing how the matrices are distributed across the compute units. The second option is more interesting: what if I use the idle CTAs to handle half-tiles via atomic operations? I'd split 44 of the 144 units into pairs, giving each pair 9 groups instead of 18, which would theoretically balance the workload better. But the math shows that even with this split, I'd still need all units to process around 13.7 groups each to avoid bottlenecks—and the overhead from atomic operations on the shared memory plus the extra zeroing required might cancel out any gains. The real cost is the atomicAdd operations on the group sums, which adds latency that probably isn't worth the marginal theoretical improvement. For implementation, I'd need to zero the shared memory buffers before the warm phase starts—that's about 72KB of zeroing, which is manageable since most CTAs are idle anyway. The unit mapping gets more complex with a 3-way split scheme, but the key insight is that the buffers need to be zeroed regardless of which IDs are assigned, so that part is straightforward. Now I'm working through the scheduling: with 432 units split across 188 CTAs, I need 3 rounds total (188+188+56), but the real question is whether this actually saves time. Each CTA processes units sequentially in a loop, and while CTAs run independently, the wall-clock time is determined by whichever CTA finishes last—so I need to figure out if the parallelism across rounds actually beats just doing all 18 group-times sequentially in a single round. I'm also exploring whether 376 units (2 per CTA) would help with load balancing, but splitting the 18 groups per tile into 9-group chunks creates 288 units that still can't distribute evenly across 188 CTAs—some get 2 units (18 groups) and others get 1 (9 groups), which brings me right back to the same 18-group wall time and adds overhead without gain. Now I'm trying a different split strategy: 88 tiles split into 3 parts of 6 groups each, and 56 tiles split into 2 parts of 9 groups each, giving exactly 376 units. If I order the units so CTAs 0–111 each get one 9-group unit plus one 6-group unit (15 groups total), and CTAs 112–187 each get two 6-group units (12 groups total), the wall time drops to 15 groups instead of the current 18—a meaningful improvement even though the imbalance persists. The real bottleneck is that every warp in a unit iterates through all its groups sequentially, so the wall time scales with group count per unit. Balancing the unit assignments is the main lever I have. I could also try widening the tiles to reduce groups per unit, which might be simpler than the complex assignment reordering. Another angle: keep 144 units but have each use 18 warps instead of 16, though that's constrained by the fixed block size. Or run two units per CTA with 8 warps each, but that just shifts the serial work around without real gains. The mixed-split approach with 376 units looks like the most promising option, though I need to weigh the error risk against the potential speedup. Before diving into that complexity, I should check whether the gateup bottleneck is truly proportional to the number of groups or if it's hitting a bandwidth floor. The math suggests there's a 16µs gap between the theoretical floor and current performance, so splitting could help close that. The same optimization applies to kda.A—I could split those tiles similarly to reduce per-CTA load. Let me start with gateup since it's the biggest phase across all four layers. For the implementation, I need to handle the split tiles carefully. The simplest approach is to split all 144 tile-units to hit the target of 376 CTAs—that's 88 tiles split into 3 and 56 split into 2. I'll order them by utilization and prezero the hg/hu output regions for the split tiles. Actually, it's cleaner to just prezero all of hg/hu upfront and make gateup always use atomicAdd, which adds negligible cost since the zeroing gets amortized across the 187 CTAs in the pick phase. For the unit table, I'm computing the mapping in-kernel from the unit index. With 56 two-split tiles and 88 three-split tiles, I can determine which tile and partition each unit belongs to, then extract the slot, gu bit, and tile index using bit operations. The ordering needs to balance the load across units. I'm checking the synchronization strategy: CTAs process units sequentially (CTA i handles unit i, then i+188), distributing the workload so that CTAs 0-111 get 15 groups total while CTAs 112-187 get 12 groups, staying within the 15-group max. The shared expert slot and down phase follow the same pattern, with the down phase showing no improvement from a 2-split approach compared to the current 8-group assignment. For numerics, splitting k in the gateup phase changes how reductions happen—instead of a single reduction over 16 warps, I'd use two atomic adds for partial sums, which introduces minor floating-point reordering but stays within tolerance (worst case 0.9966). The zero-point correction in gemv applies correctly per group even with the split, since each group's correction happens once within its unit. assistant

Done. Final state: 24.6x geomean speedup (RESULT: OK, check.py PASS), up from 19.6x at the start of this session and stable across repeat runs (24.55–24.61).

Final numbers per shape:

  • ctx 2048: 0.308 ms/tok (25.0x vs baseline's 7.69 ms)
  • ctx 8192: 0.343 ms/tok (24.5x)
  • ctx 16384: 0.381 ms/tok (24.4x)

Correctness margin is wide: worst cosine 0.9966 over seeds 0–10 × all three contexts (gate is 0.98), and the official check prints 0.9999 on every cell.

What moved the number this session, in order of impact:

  1. gemv_i4 group-loop unroll 2 (+0.7): the int4 GEMV iterated k-groups serially with only 4 outstanding loads per thread; unrolling two groups doubled memory-level parallelism. Unroll 3/4 regressed (register pressure), so 2 is the sweet spot.
  2. Adaptive attention chunking (+0.5): mla_scores/mla_ctx now pick the smallest chunk size (16/32/64/128 rows) so one round covers the cache on 188 CTAs — at ctx 2048 the old 64-row chunks left 156 CTAs idle, and at 16384 they forced a serialized second round. Softmax stats merge online per 32-row batch, and both phases derive the same chunking so the mx/sm indexing stays consistent.
  3. Warp-parallel fill_gsum (+0.4): the per-group activation sums (used by the zero-point correction) were a serial 128-element loop on 18 threads, run in every GEMV phase; now each group is a warp reduction.
  4. prefetch.global.L2::evict_last (+0.2): warming with eviction priority lets prefetched weights survive the ~30MB of expert streaming between warm and use.
  5. Small: ckv/kv_b warming in mla.A idle slots, extended pick-phase warming to the next attention layer's remaining matrices.

Things I tried and reverted after A/B (all measured worse or neutral): k-splitting gateup into 376 balanced atomic units, warming during bandwidth-heavy phases (kda.A idle, gateup idle), deeper/selective unrolls, vectorizing mla_phaseB's scale/zero loads, ctx-loop unroll. The consistent rule held: L2 warming only pays in bandwidth-idle windows.

The solution remains a single cooperative kernel launch per step() — 33 grid.sync-separated phases, 188 CTAs × 512 threads, 80KB smem — with all int4 dequant fused into the GEMVs. Stale comments were cleaned up (phase count, router description, smem sizes) and ruff passes.
